The Mu'tazila Definition of Kash Implies Human Power is Greater
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazila definition of human power, showing it leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila definition of kash implies that human power is greater than God's power.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila make humans more powerful than God by limiting God's power to one type of act.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a logical critique of the Mu'tazila concept of acquisition (kash). He shows that by their own definitions, the servant's power becomes superior to God's power, which is absurd. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of God's act
Read the first line and note that God's act is defined as origination (ibdāʿ) and bringing from non-existence. This sets the baseline.
2
See how Mu'tazila apply this to the servant
The Mu'tazila transfer this same meaning to the servant's act, making human acts also a form of creation.
3
Compare the scope of power
Maturidi argues that since the servant's power covers both good and evil acts, while God's is restricted to one type, the servant's power becomes greater. Identify this conclusion in the text.
Focus on the phrase 'further' and the nature/choice distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن معنى فعل الله هو الإبداع والإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود، وصيّرت المعتزلة ذلك معنى فعل العبد ؛ ثم جعلت للعبد قدرة على الكسب، ولم تجعل الله ؛ فصار العبد بذلك أعظم في القدرة، إذ هي تقع على مختلف الأمر من الله؛ إذ قدرته ترجع إلى أحد الوجهين ومما يبيّن [ذلك] أن كل شيء فعله نوع [واحد] جعلوه طباعا، ومن كان فعلين جعلوه اختياراً عن قدرة، فيجب في الأول كذلك. وذلك هو الحق عند المعتزلة؛ لأنهم يجعلون للعبد قدرة على منع الرب عن فعله فيما ينفي الخيرة [عنه]، ولا يجعلون مثله الله إلا أن يذهب قدرة العبد. وإذا ثبت أن في نفي خلق الأفعال تحقيق ذلك ـ وذلك مما يأباه العقل والسمع جميعا - ثبت أن الله خالق الأفعال كلها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, the meaning of God's act is origination and bringing forth from non-existence into existence, but the Mu'tazila made that the meaning of the servant's act. Then they gave the servant power over acquisition (kash) and did not give God [that]; thus the servant became greater in power thereby, since it occurs over different matters from God, since His power reduces to one of two aspects. And among what clarifies that is that everything whose act is one type they made it nature (tab'), and whoever has two acts they made it choice by power, so it must be likewise in the first. And that is the truth according to the Mu'tazila, because they give the servant power to prevent the Lord from His act in what negates choice for Him, and they do not give the like to God unless the servant's power goes away. And if it is established that negating the creation of acts realizes that—and that is something that both intellect and revelation reject—then it is established that God is the creator of all acts. And there is no power but in God.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a second argument against the Mu'tazila position that human acts are not created by God. Maturidi begins by defining God's act as origination (ibdāʿ) and bringing from non-existence to existence. The Mu'tazila, he says, applied this same meaning to the servant's act, then granted the servant power over acquisition (kash) but did not grant such power to God. Consequently, the servant becomes greater in power because his power can affect different kinds of acts, while God's power is restricted to one type. Maturidi explains that when something has only one kind of act, it is considered natural (tabʿ), but if it has two kinds of acts, it is attributed to choice based on power. Thus, according to the Mu'tazila's own logic, the servant's power would be superior as it can produce both good and evil, while God's power is limited to one type. Moreover, the Mu'tazila claim that the servant has the power to prevent God from acting in matters concerning choice, but they do not grant God a similar power over the servant's acts unless the servant's power is removed. This contradicts both reason and revelation. Therefore, Maturidi concludes that God is the creator of all acts.

Key Terms
إبداع
إخراج من العدم
كسب
قدرة
اختيار
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila concept of kash gives humans only a minor role, but Maturidi shows it actually elevates human power above God's.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly argues that the Mu'tazila position makes the servant's power greater in scope than God's, which is a serious inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows from the Mu'tazila giving the servant power over kash but not God?
A) The servant becomes equal to God.
B) The servant's power becomes greater than God's.
C) God's power becomes greater.
D) The servant has no real power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila definition of kash, when examined logically, implies that human power is superior to divine power, which is absurd and contradicts both reason and scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your power to choose between good and evil is greater than God's power, how would that change your attitude towards sin? Since this is absurd, what does this passage teach you about the true source of your ability to act—and how might that understanding lead you to greater humility and reliance on God today?
?
Do you ever find yourself acting as if you are the ultimate author of your deeds, feeling pride in your choices? How does Maturidi's logical argument—that even the ability to choose is a sign of limited power compared to God's singular, pure goodness—expose an arrogance in your heart? What specific change in your daily actions can you make to acknowledge that your power is derivative and that God alone is the true agent?

The Servant Cannot Have Power to Prevent God's Act
Maturidi exposes the Mu'tazila position as granting the servant power to prevent God's act, then affirms God as creator of all acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila view leads to the servant being able to hinder God's action, and why this necessitates affirming God as the creator of all acts.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila give humans the power to override God's will, which is blasphemy.

In the continuation of his argument, Maturidi highlights a further absurdity: the Mu'tazila claim that the servant can prevent God from acting in matters of choice. This leads to the conclusion that God does not create all acts, but Maturidi shows that reason and revelation demand the opposite.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
Read the first sentence and note that the Mu'tazila give the servant power to prevent the Lord from His act in matters concerning human choice.
2
Observe the lack of reciprocity
They do not grant God a similar power to override the servant's will unless the servant's power is removed.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Maturidi argues that since denying God's creation of acts leads to these absurdities, which intellect and revelation reject, we must affirm that God is the creator of all acts.
End with the formula 'And there is no power but in God.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن معنى فعل الله هو الإبداع والإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود، وصيّرت المعتزلة ذلك معنى فعل العبد ؛ ثم جعلت للعبد قدرة على الكسب، ولم تجعل الله ؛ فصار العبد بذلك أعظم في القدرة، إذ هي تقع على مختلف الأمر من الله؛ إذ قدرته ترجع إلى أحد الوجهين ومما يبيّن [ذلك] أن كل شيء فعله نوع [واحد] جعلوه طباعا، ومن كان فعلين جعلوه اختياراً عن قدرة، فيجب في الأول كذلك. وذلك هو الحق عند المعتزلة؛ لأنهم يجعلون للعبد قدرة على منع الرب عن فعله فيما ينفي الخيرة [عنه]، ولا يجعلون مثله الله إلا أن يذهب قدرة العبد. وإذا ثبت أن في نفي خلق الأفعال تحقيق ذلك ـ وذلك مما يأباه العقل والسمع جميعا - ثبت أن الله خالق الأفعال كلها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, the meaning of God's act is origination and bringing forth from non-existence into existence, but the Mu'tazila made that the meaning of the servant's act. Then they gave the servant power over acquisition (kash) and did not give God [that]; thus the servant became greater in power thereby, since it occurs over different matters from God, since His power reduces to one of two aspects. And among what clarifies that is that everything whose act is one type they made it nature (tab'), and whoever has two acts they made it choice by power, so it must be likewise in the first. And that is the truth according to the Mu'tazila, because they give the servant power to prevent the Lord from His act in what negates choice for Him, and they do not give the like to God unless the servant's power goes away. And if it is established that negating the creation of acts realizes that—and that is something that both intellect and revelation reject—then it is established that God is the creator of all acts. And there is no power but in God.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a second argument against the Mu'tazila position that human acts are not created by God. Maturidi begins by defining God's act as origination (ibdāʿ) and bringing from non-existence to existence. The Mu'tazila, he says, applied this same meaning to the servant's act, then granted the servant power over acquisition (kash) but did not grant such power to God. Consequently, the servant becomes greater in power because his power can affect different kinds of acts, while God's power is restricted to one type. Maturidi explains that when something has only one kind of act, it is considered natural (tabʿ), but if it has two kinds of acts, it is attributed to choice based on power. Thus, according to the Mu'tazila's own logic, the servant's power would be superior as it can produce both good and evil, while God's power is limited to one type. Moreover, the Mu'tazila claim that the servant has the power to prevent God from acting in matters concerning choice, but they do not grant God a similar power over the servant's acts unless the servant's power is removed. This contradicts both reason and revelation. Therefore, Maturidi concludes that God is the creator of all acts.

Key Terms

منع
خيرة
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila view gives humans a small role, but here Maturidi shows it gives humans power over God.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that the Mu'tazila give the servant power to prevent God from acting, which is a grave error.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say the Mu'tazila give the servant power to do?
A) Create from nothing.
B) Prevent the Lord from His act in matters of choice.
C) Act independently of God's will.
D) Choose good only.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position that God does not create human acts leads to the absurd claim that humans can overpower God, which is rejected by reason and revelation; therefore, God is the creator of all acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever behave as if your choices are outside God's control, subtly claiming power over your own life that belongs only to Him? How would your daily decisions change if you truly believed God creates all your acts?
?
When you sin, do you treat it as something you did apart from God's will, or do you recognize that even your wrongdoing occurs within His creation? How does the Maturidi position affect your sense of accountability?
?
If you fully internalized that 'there is no power but in God,' what worry or area of control would you surrender to Him today that you have been clinging to on your own?

Divine Decree

Refuting Compulsion and Affirming Acquisition (Kasb)
Maturidi refutes compulsion and explains the doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the idea of divine compulsion (jabr) and affirms human acquisition (kasb) by distinguishing between the aspect of creation and the aspect of choice.

Core Idea
God creates the act, but the servant acquires it with choice.

This passage from Imam Maturidi tackles one of the most delicate issues in Islamic theology: how can we be responsible for our actions if God creates everything? Maturidi rejects the notion that creation implies compulsion and instead explains that actions have two aspects: one from creation (God's power) and one from acquisition (human choice). Through logical reasoning and a powerful analogy, he establishes the Maturidi doctrine of kasb.

Guided Path
1
Read the original argument
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the structure: first a refutation, then a hypothetical objection, followed by clarification and analogy.
2
Identify the two perspectives
Notice how Maturidi distinguishes between the aspect of creation (khalq) and the aspect of acquisition (kasb). This dual aspect is central to his resolution.
3
Analyze the analogy of the lie
Maturidi gives an analogy: the statement of unbelief is false in itself but true as evidence of the speaker's foolishness. Similarly, an act is compelled in its creation but chosen in its acquisition.
4
Understand the role of created power
The passage ends by stating that God creates power (qudra) in the servant, and that power is the cause of his being free, not compelled. This explains how kasb works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول بإيجاب الضرورة فإنه محال فاسد، لأنه حتى أن يعلم كل أنه مختار، ولو جاز القول مما يعلمه كل على جهة قلبه الجاز ذلك في جميع العالم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فإن قلت: إذ لم يوجب الضرورة دلّ أنه لا تدبير فيه لغيرك. قيل: قد فرغنا عن دلالة ذلك؛ مع ما يجوز أن يقال: هو من طريق الخلق اضطرار، ولا ذلك الوجه إذ لا يسمى به، ومن طريق الكسب اختيار، فعلى ذلك تقسيم صنع للعبد الأمرين، وقد بيّنا. ألا ترى أن قول الكفر كذب، وهو من حيث الدلالة على سفه القائل صدق. فمثله يكون اختيارا من حيث الكسب، ومن حيث الخلق لا؛ وجهة الخلق لا تدفع عنه الاختيار بما ثبت فسواء لو كان خلق ذلك الفعل أو خلق السماء والأرض، إذ ليس في واحد صرف فعل الخلق عن الخلق، ولا إزالة الاختيار عنهم؛ فمثله خلق الأفعال. ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن تسمية الخلق لا يوجب وصف الاضطرار، إذ القدرة للفعل مخلوقة، وهي سبب جعله مختارا لا مضطرا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
As for the claim of necessitating compulsion, it is impossible and corrupt, because [if so], then everyone would know that he is not free. And if it were permissible to claim that everyone knows in his heart [that he is compelled], then that would apply to the entire world. There is no power except through God. If you say: Since he does not necessitate compulsion, it indicates that there is no administration for you by anyone else. It is said: We have already finished with the evidence of that. Moreover, it is possible to say: It is from the perspective of creation a compulsion, but from the perspective of acquisition (kasb) it is choice. Thus the division of the two matters applies to the servant, and we have explained it. Do you not see that the statement of unbelief is a lie, but from the perspective of evidence it is true regarding the foolishness of the speaker? Similarly, it is choice from the perspective of acquisition, and from the perspective of creation it is not; the aspect of creation does not negate choice because it is established. So it is the same whether He created that act or created the heavens and the earth, since in neither is there a diversion of the act of creation from creation, nor a removal of choice from them. Thus the creation of acts is similar. There is no power except through God. Moreover, naming something 'creation' does not necessitate the description of compulsion, since power (qudra) is created for the action, and it is the cause of making him free, not compelled. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God's creation of human actions implies compulsion (jabr). Maturidi argues that if that were true, then everyone would know they are compelled, which is false. He presents a hypothetical objection: if creation of actions does not imply compulsion, then it might seem that there is no divine governance in your affair. He responds that the issue has been clarified: the act is from the aspect of creation (khalq) a matter of compulsion, but from the aspect of acquisition (kasb) it is voluntary choice. This dual aspect does not negate choice. He provides an analogy: the statement of unbelief (kufr) is false as a belief but from the evidence it is true regarding the foolishness of the speaker. Similarly, an action is choice from the acquisition aspect, but from creation aspect it is not; creation does not remove choice. He emphasizes that whether God creates the act or creates the heavens, it does not change the fact that the act of creation does not divert from creation nor remove choice. Finally, he asserts that calling an act 'creation' does not imply compulsion because God creates power (qudra) in the servant, and that power is the cause of the servant being free (mukhtār) rather than compelled (muḍṭarr). This is a classic Maturidi formulation of the kasb doctrine.

Key Terms
اضطرار (compulsion)
كسب (acquisition)
اختيار (choice)
قدرة (power)
خلق (creation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi believes that humans have no choice because God creates everything.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that while God creates the act, humans acquire it with choice through the created power, thus maintaining responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does calling an act 'creation' not imply compulsion?
A) Because creation is only metaphorical.
B) Because God creates power in the servant, which causes choice.
C) Because the servant creates his own action.
D) Because compulsion only applies to non-existent actions.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi resolves the tension between divine creation and human responsibility by distinguishing the aspect of creation from the aspect of acquisition, affirming that the servant acts with choice through God-given power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know in your heart that you are free to choose, yet you also believe that all actions are created by God. How do you truly reconcile these two truths in your daily decisions? Are you ever tempted to blame God for your sins, claiming He compelled you? Reflect on a specific moment when you made a wrong choice and examine whether you truly accepted responsibility or subtly excused yourself by attributing it to divine decree. Commit now to owning your choices fully while trusting that God's creation of them does not make Him unjust.
?
God has given you power (qudra) for every action you take. But do you live as if this power is from God or from yourself? When you succeed in a good deed, do you thank God for enabling you, or do you credit your own strength? Conversely, when you fail, do you acknowledge that you misused the power He gave you, or do you feel resentful? This lesson teaches that God-given power is the cause of your freedom, not compulsion. How does that change the way you ask for His help before every action?
?
The statement of unbelief is a lie, yet it is true as evidence of the speaker's foolishness. Similarly, your sins are sins, yet they also reveal something about your heart. What do your recurring sins reveal about your understanding of God's creation and your own responsibility? Are you using God's creation of your actions as an excuse to remain complacent in sin? Tonight, before you sleep, pick one sin you have rationalized and repent sincerely, acknowledging that God created its possibility but you chose it with your own free will.

Mu'tazilite Denial of Creation of Essences
Maturidi explains the Mu'tazilite denial of creation of essences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite basis for denying that God creates the essences of things, and how Maturidi introduces the difference between the names 'Creator' and 'Speaker'.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites deny creation of essences because they only see composition and separation in the world.

Maturidi opens by noting a subtle difference in how the names 'Creator' and 'Speaker' are used in glorification. He then explains the empirical observation that led the Mu'tazilites to deny that God creates the essences of things. This passage lays the foundation for understanding their entire position on creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between 'خالق' and 'قائل'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
Find where Maturidi states the origin of the Mu'tazilite denial: they did not find any action in the witnessed world that brings something from non-existence into existence.
They only observed composition and separation.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself why this empirical observation leads them to deny that God creates the essences of things. How does this affect their view of God's power?
This connects to the broader debate about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بالخالق يخرج مخرج التعظيم، فكل ما هو أعم فهو أبلغ، وبقائل لا؛ لذلك اختلفا. فنذكر معاني إنكاره أيضاً. ثم الأصل أن إنكار المعتزلة هذا بما لم يجدوا فعل أحد يخرجه غيره من العدم إلى الوجود وهو الأصل الذي له أنكر من أنكر خلق الأعيان: بامتناعه في الشاهد عن الوجود في الحقيقة بفعل أحد، بل لا يوجد فيه غير جمع وتفريق، فأبوا أن يكون خلق أعيان الأشياء بذلك. وبمثله أنكرت المعتزلة خلق الأفعال؛ فلذلك نسبهم الأوائل إلى ذلك مع ما قولهم في التحقيق ذلك؛ لأنهم حققوا الأشياء في القدم، وجعلوا من الله إيجادها لا إحداث شيئيتها، وكانت الشيئية لا به، فيكون العالم عندهم في التحقيق حدثًا عن أشياء، لا أنه أحدث عن غير شيء. ثم ذكروا في الكفر والإيمان أنهما شيئان كان من الفاعل إيجادهما، لا جعلُها شيئين، فصارا من حيث الشيئية ليس للعبد. ثم لا ننكر ذلك، فما ننكر أن يكون من حيث الشيئية خلقا، ولا يُدفع ذلك. ولم يوجب بذلك أنه عذب لا لشيء، ولا أنه عذب للشيئية، ولا أحيل التعذيب إذا سقطت عنه الشيئية، ولا أَوْجب الشرك بين الفاعل والشيئية في العقل [و] في الوجود، ولا أطلق القول بأنه لاثنين؛ إذ هو بكليته في [حد ذاته] أنه شيء ليس له، وفي أنه إيمان وكفر له. وكذا هذا التقرير في حركة المفلوج. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The statement regarding the Creator is expressed as glorification, so whatever is more general is more eloquent, but regarding the Speaker it is not so; therefore they differ. So we mention the meanings of his denial as well. Then the origin is that the Mu'tazilite denial of this is because they did not find an action of anyone that brings it from non-existence into existence, and that is the origin due to which those who denied the creation of essences denied: because of its impossibility in the witnessed world of coming into existence in reality by the action of anyone, rather there is nothing in it except composition and separation, so they refused that the creation of the essences of things could be by that. And similarly the Mu'tazilites denied the creation of actions; therefore the early scholars attributed that to them along with what their statement is in reality; because they affirmed the reality of things in pre-eternity, and they made from God only their bringing into existence, not the bringing about of their quiddity, and quiddity was not from Him, so the world in their view is in reality originated from things, not that He originated it from nothing. Then they mentioned regarding kufr and iman that they are two things whose bringing into existence is from the agent, not His making them two things, so they became, in terms of quiddity, not belonging to the servant. Then we do not deny that; so what we deny is that they are, in terms of quiddity, creation, and that is not repelled. And that does not necessitate that He punishes for nothing, nor that He punishes for quiddity, nor is punishment invalidated if quiddity is removed from it, nor does it necessitate shirk between the agent and quiddity in intellect and in existence, nor does it permit the statement that it is from two; because in its entirety [in itself] it is a thing that is not his, and in that it is faith and disbelief for him. And likewise this exposition applies to the movement of the paralyzed. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Mu'tazilite position. Maturidi begins by stating that the name 'Creator' is used for glorification, so the more general it is, the more eloquent, but 'Speaker' is not like that. He then explains the root of the Mu'tazilite denial: they observe that in the witnessed world, no one brings something from non-existence into existence; only composition and separation occur. Therefore they deny that God creates the essences of things. Similarly, they deny the creation of actions. They hold that the quiddity of things is pre-eternal, and God only brings them into existence, not creating their quiddity. Consequently, the world is originated from pre-existing things, not from nothing. Regarding kufr and iman, they consider them as two things whose existence is from the agent (God), but not their being two things. So in terms of quiddity, they are not the servant's. Maturidi responds that he does not deny that; what he denies is that they are, in terms of quiddity, a creation. He then argues that this does not lead to problematic conclusions like punishment for nothing, or shirk, etc. The example of the paralyzed person's movement is given as analogous. The passage concludes with a formulaic 'La quwwata illa billah.'

Key Terms
تعظيم
خالق
قائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what empirical observation leads the Mu'tazilites to deny that God creates the essences of things?
A) They see that everything changes over time
B) They see that in the witnessed world, no action brings something from non-existence into existence
C) They see that humans create their own actions
D) They see that God's power is unlimited

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites deny that God creates the essences of things because they only observe composition and separation in the physical world, not genuine creation from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu‘tazilites relied solely on what they could observe in the physical world to decide what is possible for God. In your own life, where have you allowed your limited experience to set boundaries on what you believe God can do? Will you repent of this and surrender to Allah's absolute power, even when it defies your observation?
?
When you witness pain or evil in the world, do you ever find yourself subconsciously thinking that God could not have willed or created that particular event because it seems too harsh? How does this lesson's reminder about the Mu‘tazilite error challenge you to trust that Allah creates everything—even the difficult—with wisdom? What step will you take to strengthen that trust this week?
?
The Mu‘tazilites saw only composition and separation, missing the deeper reality of divine creation. What 'compositions and separations' in your daily life—such as events that seem like mere coincidence or human effort—are you overlooking as the direct creation of Allah? How can you train your heart to see His hand more clearly?

Mu'tazilite View on Creation of Actions and Quiddity
Maturidi explains the Mu'tazilite view on creation of actions and quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position on the creation of actions and their concept of quiddity as pre-eternal, and how Maturidi characterizes their view as making the world originated from things rather than from nothing.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites believe quiddity is pre-eternal; God only brings things into existence, not their quiddity.

Maturidi continues by explaining that the Mu'tazilites extended their denial to the creation of actions. They held that the quiddity (essence) of things is pre-eternal and not created by God. Instead, God only brings things into existence. This leads to the view that the world is originated from pre-existing things, not from nothing. This passage reveals a key metaphysical commitment of the Mu'tazilites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the terms 'إيجاد' (bringing into existence) and 'إحداث شيئيتها' (bringing about their quiddity).
These two concepts are crucial to the Mu'tazilite distinction.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite view on actions
The Mu'tazilites denied the creation of actions just as they denied the creation of essences. They affirm the reality of things in pre-eternity and only assign 'bringing into existence' to God.
3
Analyze the consequence
Maturidi says that in their view, the world is 'originated from things' (ḥadathan ʿan ashyā') not from nothing. Consider how this differs from the orthodox view of creation ex nihilo.
This is a major theological divergence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بالخالق يخرج مخرج التعظيم، فكل ما هو أعم فهو أبلغ، وبقائل لا؛ لذلك اختلفا. فنذكر معاني إنكاره أيضاً. ثم الأصل أن إنكار المعتزلة هذا بما لم يجدوا فعل أحد يخرجه غيره من العدم إلى الوجود وهو الأصل الذي له أنكر من أنكر خلق الأعيان: بامتناعه في الشاهد عن الوجود في الحقيقة بفعل أحد، بل لا يوجد فيه غير جمع وتفريق، فأبوا أن يكون خلق أعيان الأشياء بذلك. وبمثله أنكرت المعتزلة خلق الأفعال؛ فلذلك نسبهم الأوائل إلى ذلك مع ما قولهم في التحقيق ذلك؛ لأنهم حققوا الأشياء في القدم، وجعلوا من الله إيجادها لا إحداث شيئيتها، وكانت الشيئية لا به، فيكون العالم عندهم في التحقيق حدثًا عن أشياء، لا أنه أحدث عن غير شيء. ثم ذكروا في الكفر والإيمان أنهما شيئان كان من الفاعل إيجادهما، لا جعلُها شيئين، فصارا من حيث الشيئية ليس للعبد. ثم لا ننكر ذلك، فما ننكر أن يكون من حيث الشيئية خلقا، ولا يُدفع ذلك. ولم يوجب بذلك أنه عذب لا لشيء، ولا أنه عذب للشيئية، ولا أحيل التعذيب إذا سقطت عنه الشيئية، ولا أَوْجب الشرك بين الفاعل والشيئية في العقل [و] في الوجود، ولا أطلق القول بأنه لاثنين؛ إذ هو بكليته في [حد ذاته] أنه شيء ليس له، وفي أنه إيمان وكفر له. وكذا هذا التقرير في حركة المفلوج. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The statement regarding the Creator is expressed as glorification, so whatever is more general is more eloquent, but regarding the Speaker it is not so; therefore they differ. So we mention the meanings of his denial as well. Then the origin is that the Mu'tazilite denial of this is because they did not find an action of anyone that brings it from non-existence into existence, and that is the origin due to which those who denied the creation of essences denied: because of its impossibility in the witnessed world of coming into existence in reality by the action of anyone, rather there is nothing in it except composition and separation, so they refused that the creation of the essences of things could be by that. And similarly the Mu'tazilites denied the creation of actions; therefore the early scholars attributed that to them along with what their statement is in reality; because they affirmed the reality of things in pre-eternity, and they made from God only their bringing into existence, not the bringing about of their quiddity, and quiddity was not from Him, so the world in their view is in reality originated from things, not that He originated it from nothing. Then they mentioned regarding kufr and iman that they are two things whose bringing into existence is from the agent, not His making them two things, so they became, in terms of quiddity, not belonging to the servant. Then we do not deny that; so what we deny is that they are, in terms of quiddity, creation, and that is not repelled. And that does not necessitate that He punishes for nothing, nor that He punishes for quiddity, nor is punishment invalidated if quiddity is removed from it, nor does it necessitate shirk between the agent and quiddity in intellect and in existence, nor does it permit the statement that it is from two; because in its entirety [in itself] it is a thing that is not his, and in that it is faith and disbelief for him. And likewise this exposition applies to the movement of the paralyzed. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Mu'tazilite position. Maturidi begins by stating that the name 'Creator' is used for glorification, so the more general it is, the more eloquent, but 'Speaker' is not like that. He then explains the root of the Mu'tazilite denial: they observe that in the witnessed world, no one brings something from non-existence into existence; only composition and separation occur. Therefore they deny that God creates the essences of things. Similarly, they deny the creation of actions. They hold that the quiddity of things is pre-eternal, and God only brings them into existence, not creating their quiddity. Consequently, the world is originated from pre-existing things, not from nothing. Regarding kufr and iman, they consider them as two things whose existence is from the agent (God), but not their being two things. So in terms of quiddity, they are not the servant's. Maturidi responds that he does not deny that; what he denies is that they are, in terms of quiddity, a creation. He then argues that this does not lead to problematic conclusions like punishment for nothing, or shirk, etc. The example of the paralyzed person's movement is given as analogous. The passage concludes with a formulaic 'La quwwata illa billah.'

Key Terms
شيئية
إيجاد
إحداث
أعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the Mu'tazilites believe about the quiddity of things?
A) Quiddity is created by God
B) Quiddity is pre-eternal and not from God
C) Quiddity is created by humans
D) Quiddity does not exist

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites believe that quiddity is pre-eternal and uncreated; God only brings things into existence, resulting in a world originated from pre-existing things rather than from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing in this world—not even your own nature or the nature of your circumstances—exists independently of Allah's creative act? Where in your life do you unconsciously act as if some 'given' reality is outside His will?
?
The Mu'tazilites subtly diminished God's power by separating quiddity from His creation. In what subtle ways might you be limiting God's sovereignty over your life, perhaps by thinking certain traits or situations are simply 'the way things are' rather than directly from Him?
?
If God did not create the very essence of things, then the world is not fully dependent on Him. How does this challenge you to reflect on your own total dependence on Allah for every aspect of your being, including your own self? What would change in your daily reliance on Him if you truly internalized that He is the Creator of everything including quiddity?

Mu'tazilite Position on Kufr and Iman and Maturidi's Response
Maturidi examines and responds to the Mu'tazilite view on kufr and iman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite view that kufr and iman are things whose existence is from God but whose quiddity belongs to the servant, and Maturidi's argument that this does not lead to problematic conclusions.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites separate the quiddity of actions from their existence, attributing only existence to God.

Maturidi addresses the Mu'tazilite position on kufr (disbelief) and iman (faith). They consider these as two things whose existence comes from God, but their being two things (i.e., their quiddity) is not from God. Thus, in terms of quiddity, they are not the servant's. Maturidi responds by clarifying what he denies and argues that this stance does not lead to punishment for nothing, shirk, or other absurdities. He uses the example of a paralyzed person's movement to illustrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic passage from 'ثم ذكروا' to the end. Notice the nuanced distinctions between 'إيجاد' and 'جعل'.
The terms 'شيئية' (quiddity) and 'خالق' (creator) are key.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position on kufr and iman
They say that kufr and iman are two things whose bringing into existence (ijad) is from God, but He did not make them two things (ja'l). So in terms of quiddity, they are not the servant's.
3
Analyze Maturidi's response
Maturidi states that he does not deny that; but he denies that they are, in terms of quiddity, a creation. He then argues that this does not necessitate punishment for nothing, nor shirk, nor attributing actions to two agents.
He uses the example of a paralyzed person's movement to show that the quiddity can be absent while the action is still attributed to the agent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God does not create the quiddity of actions, then He cannot punish or reward them, or that this leads to associating partners with God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that none of these consequences follow: punishment is for the action itself, not the quiddity, and shirk is avoided because quiddity is not a separate agent.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following does NOT follow from the Mu'tazilite position on kufr and iman?
A) That God punishes for nothing
B) That God punishes for the quiddity itself
C) That the act can be attributed to two agents
D) That the servant is the sole creator of his actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazilite separation of quiddity from existence does not lead to absurd consequences, and the act remains attributable to the servant for reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the Mu'tazilite-Maturidi debate about the quiddity of actions affect your own understanding of your sins? If the very 'thing-ness' of a sin is not created by you, why do you deserve punishment? Read the passage again and ask yourself: Do I truly believe that every sin I commit is ultimately created by God, yet I am fully accountable? How does that change the way you feel about your past mistakes and seek forgiveness?
?
Maturidi uses the analogy of a paralyzed person's movement: the quiddity is absent yet the movement is still attributed. Think of a situation where you felt powerless over a wrong action you committed. In light of this lesson, what does that say about your responsibility? Are there areas in your life where you have denied ownership of your actions, blaming circumstance or your nature, while God's creation of the act remains? Commit today to accept full moral responsibility for one specific action you have been excusing.
?
The Mu'tazilites fear that attributing the quiddity of sin to God would make Him unjust. Maturidi responds that God's punishment is not for the quiddity but for the act as a whole. Ask yourself: Do you secretly doubt God's justice when you see evil in the world? How does this lesson's reasoning—that the quiddity is not the ground for punishment—help you trust that God's judgment is perfectly just, even when you cannot see how? Write down one doubt you have about divine justice and then read the source translation again to find Maturidi's counterargument.

Refuting al-Ka'bi on the Basis of Blame
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that blame based on sin would necessitate blaming all actions, including good ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attaching blame to the aspect of sin leads to absurd consequences, and how Maturidi uses this to defend divine justice.

Core Idea
Blame cannot be attached to sin without also blaming faith and goodness.

This lesson begins with al-Ka'bi's claim that the doer of sin is more blameworthy than its Creator. Maturidi responds with a powerful logical counter: if blame were based on the aspect of sinfulness, then every act sharing that aspect would be blameworthy, including faith and virtue. This refutation sets the stage for a deeper understanding of how acts relate to God and human beings.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's claim and Maturidi's response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: a claim followed by a rhetorical question.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Maturidi argues that if blame were tied to the aspect of sin, then all acts would be blameworthy, including faith. Find this part in the text.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the logic
Why cannot blame be absolute? Consider that blame must be directed appropriately. The passage concludes that aspects must be assigned to what fits them.
4
Connect to the larger argument
This refutation clears the way for Maturidi's dual-aspect theory. It shows that blame is not inherent in the act's creation but in its relation to the servant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبي : ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذم من خالقها . قيل له : وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية؛ وأنه خلافٌ للعبد والله وغير لهما، وأنه حجة الله ودليل سفه الكافر؛ فإن الذمّ لشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمّى به؛ فيجب الذم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات، [ف] يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. ثم الذي من الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعلُه في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورًا ومذموماً، وهو من هذا الوجه حق وحكمة، والفعل من حيث العبد سفه وجور، ومن ذلك الوجه قبيح ومعصية. ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الكافر على ما هو عنده كان جاهلاً ومن أخبر به كان كاذبًا، ومن عرفه على ما عليه حقيقته كان عالما حكيمًا، ولو أخبر به كان صادقا. فعلى ذلك خلق الله ذلك وجعله على ماهو عليه فعل العبد. وعلى قول من يجعل خلق الشيء غيره لا معنى له، لأن فعل الله في الحقيقة ليس بكفر ولا جور ولا سفه ولا الذي كان من العبد من خضوع وذلة وطاعة ومعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نعارض بتسميته غير الذي خلق الموت وأحوال الخلق أحق من الذي خلق، وهو بالجملة مُسمّى أنه خالق ذلك. فمهما قال في ذلك فهو جواب له في الأول.
Then al-Ka'bi said: 'What makes the doer of sin more deserving of blame than its Creator?' It was said to him: 'And what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than the aspects of quiddity, movement, origination, and accident? And that it is contrary to the servant and God and other than them both, and that it is God's proof and evidence of the disbeliever's folly, for blame for any of that would necessitate blame for every named thing; then blame would be obligatory for the act of faith and every good act, and if it is not obligatory, then aspects are established for it, so it is directed to whatever is appropriate to it. Then that which is from God Almighty is wisdom in terms of His making it in reality ugly, folly, injustice, and blameworthy, yet in this respect it is truth and wisdom; and the act from the servant's perspective is folly and injustice, and in that respect it is ugly and disobedience. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of the disbeliever as it is according to himself is ignorant, and whoever reports it is a liar, and whoever knows it as it truly is is wise, and if he reports it he is truthful. Thus God created that and made it as it is, the act of the servant. And according to the statement of one who makes creation of something other than it, it has no meaning, because the act of God in reality is not disbelief, nor injustice, nor folly, nor that which was from the servant of humility, lowliness, obedience, and disobedience. And there is no power except with God. Then we counter by naming other than Him as the one who created death and the states of creation—more deserving than the one who created, and in summary, He is named the Creator of that. So whatever he says about that, it is his answer for the first.'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's statement that the doer of sin is more deserving of blame than its Creator (God). Maturidi responds by questioning why the aspect of sin should be more blameworthy than other aspects like quiddity, movement, etc. He argues that if blame were attached to these aspects, then it would also apply to faith and good deeds, which is absurd. He then explains the dual aspect: from God, the act is created as an instance of wisdom and justice, even though it is ugly and blameworthy in itself; from the servant, it is folly and sin. The example of the disbeliever's act shows that knowing it as it truly is requires recognizing both aspects. God creates the act as it is—the act of the servant—and God's act in reality is not kufr or injustice. Finally, a counter-argument is presented: if one claims that something other than God is the creator of death and states, then that other would be more deserving of being called creator, but in summary God is called the Creator of that. Whatever al-Ka'bi says in response will be his own answer to the first objection.

Key Terms
معصية
ذم
خالق
فاعل
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if blame were attached to the aspect of sin?
A) Only sinful acts would be blameworthy.
B) Blame would apply to every named thing, including faith.
C) God would be directly blameworthy.
D) Only the servant would be blameworthy.

Key Takeaway
Blame cannot be based on the aspect of sin without leading to absurdity; therefore, the blameworthiness of sin must be relative to the human agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever find yourself blaming your circumstances, your environment, or even God for it? How does this lesson's distinction between creation and acquisition force you to take full responsibility?
?
If you truly believed that the aspect of sin in an act comes only from your own acquisition and not from God's creative will, how would your repentance and resolve to avoid sin change starting today?
?
Consider the absurdity Maturidi exposes: if blame were based on the simple presence of sin as a quality, even faith would be blameworthy. Does your heart ever secretly feel that God is somehow responsible for your sins? What would it mean for your relationship with Him to let go of that blame entirely?

6 min
The Dual Aspect of Actions: Wisdom and Sin
Maturidi explains the dual aspect of actions and illustrates with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dual-aspect theory: that the same act is wisdom from God's perspective and sin from the human perspective.

Core Idea
An act can be both God's wise creation and human sin.

Having refuted the simplistic blame attribution, Maturidi now introduces a nuanced framework: every action has two aspects—one relating to God as Creator, the other to the human as agent. From God, the act is wisdom and truth; from the human, it may be folly and disobedience. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for understanding divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the dual-aspect statement
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi states that from God the act is wisdom, and from the servant it is folly and sin.
2
Analyze the example of the disbeliever's act
Maturidi gives an example: knowing the act as the disbeliever sees it is ignorance; true knowledge sees it as it truly is. Reflect on what this illustrates.
3
Understand God's act of creation
Maturidi clarifies that God's creation of the act is not itself kufr or injustice; it is the servant's act that carries those qualities.
4
Examine the counter-argument
The passage ends with a counter: if someone says something else creates death, that other would be more deserving of the name 'creator,' but in summary, God is the Creator of all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبي : ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذم من خالقها . قيل له : وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية؛ وأنه خلافٌ للعبد والله وغير لهما، وأنه حجة الله ودليل سفه الكافر؛ فإن الذمّ لشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمّى به؛ فيجب الذم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات، [ف] يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. ثم الذي من الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعلُه في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورًا ومذموماً، وهو من هذا الوجه حق وحكمة، والفعل من حيث العبد سفه وجور، ومن ذلك الوجه قبيح ومعصية. ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الكافر على ما هو عنده كان جاهلاً ومن أخبر به كان كاذبًا، ومن عرفه على ما عليه حقيقته كان عالما حكيمًا، ولو أخبر به كان صادقا. فعلى ذلك خلق الله ذلك وجعله على ماهو عليه فعل العبد. وعلى قول من يجعل خلق الشيء غيره لا معنى له، لأن فعل الله في الحقيقة ليس بكفر ولا جور ولا سفه ولا الذي كان من العبد من خضوع وذلة وطاعة ومعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نعارض بتسميته غير الذي خلق الموت وأحوال الخلق أحق من الذي خلق، وهو بالجملة مُسمّى أنه خالق ذلك. فمهما قال في ذلك فهو جواب له في الأول.
Then al-Ka'bi said: 'What makes the doer of sin more deserving of blame than its Creator?' It was said to him: 'And what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than the aspects of quiddity, movement, origination, and accident? And that it is contrary to the servant and God and other than them both, and that it is God's proof and evidence of the disbeliever's folly, for blame for any of that would necessitate blame for every named thing; then blame would be obligatory for the act of faith and every good act, and if it is not obligatory, then aspects are established for it, so it is directed to whatever is appropriate to it. Then that which is from God Almighty is wisdom in terms of His making it in reality ugly, folly, injustice, and blameworthy, yet in this respect it is truth and wisdom; and the act from the servant's perspective is folly and injustice, and in that respect it is ugly and disobedience. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of the disbeliever as it is according to himself is ignorant, and whoever reports it is a liar, and whoever knows it as it truly is is wise, and if he reports it he is truthful. Thus God created that and made it as it is, the act of the servant. And according to the statement of one who makes creation of something other than it, it has no meaning, because the act of God in reality is not disbelief, nor injustice, nor folly, nor that which was from the servant of humility, lowliness, obedience, and disobedience. And there is no power except with God. Then we counter by naming other than Him as the one who created death and the states of creation—more deserving than the one who created, and in summary, He is named the Creator of that. So whatever he says about that, it is his answer for the first.'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's statement that the doer of sin is more deserving of blame than its Creator (God). Maturidi responds by questioning why the aspect of sin should be more blameworthy than other aspects like quiddity, movement, etc. He argues that if blame were attached to these aspects, then it would also apply to faith and good deeds, which is absurd. He then explains the dual aspect: from God, the act is created as an instance of wisdom and justice, even though it is ugly and blameworthy in itself; from the servant, it is folly and sin. The example of the disbeliever's act shows that knowing it as it truly is requires recognizing both aspects. God creates the act as it is—the act of the servant—and God's act in reality is not kufr or injustice. Finally, a counter-argument is presented: if one claims that something other than God is the creator of death and states, then that other would be more deserving of being called creator, but in summary God is called the Creator of that. Whatever al-Ka'bi says in response will be his own answer to the first objection.

حكمة
سفه
جور
خلق
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God creates the sin, then He is to blame.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between the act as creation (from God) and as acquisition (from the servant), each with its own moral aspect. God's act of creation is wisdom, not sin.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, from which perspective is the act of disobedience considered wisdom and truth?
A) From the perspective of the servant
B) From the perspective of God
C) From the perspective of the Mu'tazilites
D) From the perspective of the angels

Key Takeaway
The same act is wisdom from God and sin from the human; blame attaches to the human's acquisition, not to God's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you next commit a sin, will you pause to recognize that the same act you are blaming yourself for is also part of God's wise decree? How does that knowledge change the way you repent and seek forgiveness?
?
Have you ever secretly resented God for a trial that was caused by your own sin? After this lesson, are you willing to accept full responsibility for your acquisition while still trusting in God's wisdom? What would that look like in your prayer tonight?
?
If you truly believed that every sin you commit is simultaneously an act of divine wisdom, would you still be as quick to despair? Or would you find hope that God can use even your worst failures for good? What is one specific sin you need to reframe in this light today?

The Thing-ness of Human Actions: Divine Creation and Human Accountability
Maturidi explains that thing-ness of actions is from God, not invalidating accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi teaches that the 'thing-ness' of human actions (their existence as created entities) belongs to God, and how this does not undermine human accountability or lead to theological errors.

Core Idea
The existence of an action is from God; its moral quality is from man.

Imam Maturidi addresses a subtle but crucial point in Qadar: the distinction between the mere existence of an action and its moral attribution. He argues that the thing-ness (شيئية) of an action—its character as something real—is created by God, not the servant. This might seem to raise questions about punishment and shirk. In this passage, Maturidi clarifies that this view actually preserves both divine sovereignty and human responsibility, using the example of a paralyzed person's movement to illustrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the rhythm of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the contrast between 'شيئية' (thing-ness) and the moral attributes of the action (faith/disbelief). Maturidi assigns thing-ness to God and moral quality to the servant.
3
List the denied implications
Maturidi denies five consequences of his view: arbitrary punishment, punishment for thing-ness, impossibility of punishment if thing-ness removed, shirk between agent and thing-ness, and dual agency. Write them down.
These denials show what his view does NOT entail.
4
Reflect on the paralyzed person analogy
Consider how the movement of a paralyzed person illustrates that the thing-ness of an action is from God, but the moral responsibility (if any) attaches to the servant's intention.
This analogy helps distinguish between creation and acquisition.
5
Connect to the learning objective
Summarize how this teaching protects both divine omnipotence and human accountability without falling into error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا جعلُها شيئين، فصارا من حيث الشيئية ليس للعبد. ثم لا ننكر ذلك، فما ننكر أن يكون من حيث الشيئية خلقا، ولا يُدفع ذلك. ولم يوجب بذلك أنه عذب لا لشيء، ولا أنه عذب للشيئية، ولا أحيل التعذيب إذا سقطت عنه الشيئية، ولا أَوْجب الشرك بين الفاعل والشيئية في العقل [و] في الوجود، ولا أطلق القول بأنه لاثنين؛ إذ هو بكليته في [حد ذاته] أنه شيء ليس له، وفي أنه إيمان وكفر له. وكذا هذا التقرير في حركة المفلوج. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He did not make them two things, so they became from the aspect of thing-ness not belonging to the servant. Then we do not deny that, so we do not deny that from the aspect of thing-ness it is creation, and that is not repelled. And that does not necessitate that He punishes for no reason, nor that He punishes for thing-ness, nor is punishment impossible if thing-ness is removed from it, nor does it necessitate association between the agent and thing-ness in intellect and existence, nor does it release the statement that it is from two; for it is in its entirety in itself that it is a thing not belonging to him, and that it is faith and disbelief belonging to him. And likewise this explanation in the movement of the paralyzed. And there is no power but with God.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation distinguishing between the aspect of 'thing-ness' (شيئية) of an action and the action itself as attributed to the human agent. Maturidi argues that the thing-ness of an action—its existence as a created entity—is from God, not from the servant. However, this does not lead to problematic conclusions: it does not mean that God punishes for no reason or that punishment is invalid if thing-ness is removed. It also does not imply polytheism (شرك) by having two creators. The action in its entirety is a thing not belonging to the servant in terms of its creation, yet it is faith or disbelief belonging to the servant in terms of acquisition. Maturidi gives the example of the movement of a paralyzed person to illustrate that the thing-ness of the movement is from God, but the moral responsibility attaches to the servant's intention or choice.

Key Terms
شيئية
فعل العبد
خلق
تعذيب
شرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates the existence of human actions, then man cannot be held accountable, and punishment becomes arbitrary or impossible.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the thing-ness (existence) of an action is from God, but the action's moral quality (faith or disbelief) is acquired by the servant, so accountability remains. Punishment is for the moral quality, not for the existence, and it is not arbitrary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, to whom does the 'thing-ness' (شيئية) of an action belong?
A) The human agent exclusively
B) God
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The thing-ness (existence) of every action is created by God alone, but the action's moral character (faith/disbelief) is acquired by the servant, preserving both divine unity and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that God 'created' your sin, and thus part of its blame belongs to Him? How does Maturidi's distinction between thing-ness and moral quality confront that excuse? Will you now take full responsibility for your actions, acknowledging that their existence is from God but their moral quality is your acquisition?
?
Your faith and disbelief are described as 'belonging to you' in this passage. Are you actively cultivating your faith as your own, or do you passively assume it is from God alone? What specific step will you take today to own your faith—not just as a created thing, but as a personal act of submission?
?
The paralyzed person's movement is used as an example. Think of a situation where you felt powerless over your actions—a habit, an addiction, a recurring sin. How does knowing that the 'thing-ness' of that action is from God, yet the moral quality is yours, change your approach to overcoming it? What will you do now that you cannot blame your circumstances?

Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Blame for Sin
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that the doer of sin is more blamable than its creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that blame for sin falls on the human's moral choice, not on God's creation of the act.

Core Idea
Blame attaches to the human aspect of sin, not to God's act of creation.

Al-Ka'bi raised a theological challenge: if God creates every act, including sins, why is the human doer more blamable than God? Maturidi responds by distinguishing between the aspect of creation (which is from God and wise) and the aspect of moral quality (which belongs to the human). This lesson unpacks that nuanced response and its implications for divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection by al-Ka'bi
Start by reading the first line where al-Ka'bi questions why the doer is more blamable than the creator. Then read the full passage and its translation to get the complete argument.
2
Identify the response's key distinction
Notice how the response shifts from blame on the act itself to blame on the 'aspect' (جهة) of sin belonging to the human. Maturidi argues that if blame were on creation, even good acts would be blameworthy.
Look for the phrase 'جهة المعصية'.
3
Analyze the example about knowledge
Study the example comparing the ignorant disbeliever and the wise knower. Maturidi uses this to show that the same act can be seen differently depending on the aspect considered.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that God's creation of the act is not itself disbelief or injustice; those qualities come only from the human's acquisition. This refutes al-Ka'bi's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبي: ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذم من خالقها. قيل له: وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية؛ وأنه خلافٌ للعبد والله وغير لهما، وأنه حجة الله ودليل سفه الكافر؛ فإن الذمّ لشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمّى به؛ فيجب الذم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات، [ف] يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. ثم الذي من الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعلُه في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورًا ومذموماً، وهو من هذا الوجه حق وحكمة، والفعل من حيث العبد سفه وجور، ومن ذلك الوجه قبيح ومعصية. ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الكافر على ما هو عنده كان جاهلاً ومن أخبر به كان كاذبًا، ومن عرفه على ما عليه حقيقته كان عالما حكيمًا، ولو أخبر به كان صادقا. فعلى ذلك خلق الله ذلك وجعله على ماهو عليه فعل العبد. وعلى قول من يجعل خلق الشيء غيره لا معنى له، لأن فعل الله في الحقيقة ليس بكفر ولا جور ولا سفه ولا الذي كان من العبد من خضوع وذلة وطاعة ومعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then al-Ka'bi said: What makes the doer of sin more deserving of blame than its creator? It was said to him: And what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than the aspect of thing-ness, movement, event-ness, and accident-ness? And that it is different for the servant and God and other than them both, and that it is God's proof and evidence of the ignorance of the disbeliever? For blame for something from that would necessitate blame for every name by it; thus blame would be necessary upon the act of faith and every good, and if it is not necessary, then aspects are established for it, so it is directed to everything befitting it. Then that which is from God, the Exalted, is wisdom from the aspect of His making it in reality ugly, foolish, unjust, and blameworthy, and from this aspect it is true and wisdom, and the act from the aspect of the servant is foolish and unjust, and from that aspect it is ugly and disobedience. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of the disbeliever as it is according to him is ignorant, and whoever informs of it is a liar, and whoever knows it as it is in its reality is knowledgeable and wise, and if he informed of it he would be truthful. Thus, God created that and made it as it is the act of the servant. And according to the saying of one who makes the creation of a thing other than it, it has no meaning, because the act of God in reality is not disbelief, nor injustice, nor foolishness, nor that which was from the servant of humility, lowliness, obedience, and disobedience. And there is no power but with God.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Ka'bi and the response. Al-Ka'bi questions why the human doer of sin is more blamable than God who creates the sin. The response argues that blame attaches to the specific aspect of the act that belongs to the human—the moral quality—not to the aspect of creation (thing-ness, movement, etc.). If blame were attached to the created aspect, then even faith and good deeds would be blameworthy, which is absurd. Instead, aspects are distinguished: from God's perspective, the creation of the act is wise and just, even though the act itself is ugly from the human perspective. The response uses the example of knowledge: knowing the act as the disbeliever does leads to ignorance, but knowing it as it truly is leads to wisdom. Thus, God's creation of the act does not make Him blameworthy; the blame falls on the human for his evil choice.

فاعل المعصية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The doer of sin is more blamable because God created the sin directly, so He shares blame.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that blame attaches to the aspect of the act that belongs to the human's choice, not to God's act of creation. God's creation is wise and just, and the moral quality is acquired by the human.

D) Because the human's will is completely independent

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that blame for sin belongs to the human's moral aspect, not to God's creation of the act.

?

The Reality of Human Action and Divine Creation
Maturidi argues for the reality of human choice alongside divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions are real and chosen, and that divine creation does not negate human freedom or moral accountability.

Core Idea
God's creation of actions does not compel the human; choice remains real.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key question of Islamic theology: if God creates everything, including human actions, how can humans be held responsible? In this passage, he establishes that human actions are genuine and chosen, and that God's creation, knowledge, and decree coexist with human freedom. This principle ensures the validity of divine command, prohibition, and reward.

Guided Path
1
Read and translate the passage
Read the Arabic source and the English translation carefully to grasp the overall argument. Notice the flow from objection to principle to implications.
The passage starts with a counterargument structure, so pay attention to 'Then we counter...'.
2
Identify the core principle
Locate where Maturidi states that an act is established for the servant in reality and that he has choice. This is the anchor of the entire discussion.
Look for the phrase 'an act has been established for the servant in reality, and that he has choice regarding it'.
3
Analyze the relationship with divine creation
Understand how Maturidi explains that divine creation does not push, force, or compel the human. See the list of divine actions (knowledge, informing, writing in the Preserved Tablet) that coexist without compulsion.
The phrase 'since He did not compel him to his act nor force him to it' is key.
4
Apply to moral accountability
Reflect on the conclusion: because the human is not compelled, command, prohibition, punishment, and reward are all good and consistent. This shows that divine decree and human responsibility are compatible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نعارض بتسميته غير الذي خلق الموت وأحوال الخلق أحق من الذي خلق، وهو بالجملة مُسمّى أنه خالق ذلك. فمهما قال في ذلك فهو جواب له في الأول. والأصل أنه قد ثبت للعبد فعل في الحقيقة، وأنه له مختار، وأنه أَثَر الأشياء عنده وأحبها، وأن خلق ذلك لم يدفعه إليه، ولم يحمله ولم يضطره إليه. فوجود ذلك ووجود علمه به وخبره عنه وإثباته في اللوح المحفوظ وإيجاب معاودته لوقت فعله وتسميته بما سمّى ـ إذ لم يضطره إلى فعله ولا حمله عليه ـ حسن معه الأمر والنهي والتعذيب والإثابة. ومن أنكر بهذا خلقه فتعلقه بهذا النوع خيال. وحقه أن ينظر في الوجه الذي به يُعرف خلق الأشياء، فإن أمكن تحقيقه فالإنكار بهذا النوع إنما هو جهل بالحكمة. وعلى ذلك كان أول ما جبل عليه. فسيعلم إن خضع للمكرم به إن شاء الله، وإن لم يمكن تسقط المسألة، ويُفصل الذي عارض به كلُّه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then we counter with His naming the one who created death and the states of creation is more deserving than the one who created, and in summary he is named that he is the creator of that. So whatever he says about that, it is an answer to him in the first. And the principle is that an act has been established for the servant in reality, and that he has choice regarding it, and that he is the effect of things according to him and their most beloved, and that the creation of that did not push it to him, nor did it force him, nor did it compel him to it. So the existence of that and the existence of His knowledge of it and His inform about it and His establishing it in the Preserved Tablet and necessitating its recurrence at the time of its doing and His naming it as He named—since He did not compel him to his act nor force him to it—is good along with command, prohibition, punishment, and reward. And whoever denies this creation [by God] and attaches himself to this type [of argument] is delusion. And his duty is to look into the aspect by which the creation of things is known; if its verification is possible, then denial of this type is only ignorance of wisdom. And upon that was the first thing he was created upon. So he will know—if he submits to the one honored by it, God willing—and if it is not possible, the question falls, and everything he objected with is set aside. And there is no power but with God.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle that human actions are real and the human has genuine choice (اختيار). God's creation of the act does not remove the human's agency; rather, God's knowledge, decree, and writing in the Preserved Tablet coexist with human freedom because God does not compel the human. Therefore, commanding, prohibiting, punishing, and rewarding are all consistent. Maturidi warns that denying this relationship is delusion and ignorance of wisdom. The opponent's objections are dismissed as not based on proper understanding of divine creation.

Key Terms
فعل
مختار
خلق
جبر
لوح محفوظ
أمر ونهي
ثواب وعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's creation of actions means humans are compelled and have no free will.
Correction: Maturidi explains that God's creation does not compel or force humans to act; the human still has genuine choice and is responsible.

Checkpoint

A) Human actions are illusions
B) Human actions are real and the human has choice, and God's creation does not compel
C) God creates all actions and humans have no role
D) Humans create their own actions independently

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that human actions are real and chosen, and God's creation does not compel, thus preserving moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Human Action, Divine Decree, and Moral Responsibility
Maturidi establishes the reality of human action and its compatibility with divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi reconciles genuine human choice with divine creation and command.

Core Idea
Human actions are real and chosen, yet created by God without compulsion.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a central question in Islamic theology: How can humans be held accountable if God creates all actions? Maturidi establishes that the servant has genuine choice and real action, and that God's creation of that action does not imply compulsion. This makes divine commands and punishments meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dense argument style of Maturidi.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' emphasizes reliance on God.
2
Identify the key claims
Look for claims: the servant has real action (fi‘l), choice (mukhtar), and that God's creation did not compel him. Note how these are presented as established principles.
3
Understand the link to divine command
The passage ties the reality of human choice to the appropriateness of command, prohibition, punishment, and reward. See how the Preserved Tablet fits in.
The phrase 'حسن معه الأمر والنهي' shows the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the objection and conclusion
Maturidi counters those who deny this, calling it illusion or ignorance of wisdom. The closing 'فسيعلم إن خضع' suggests submission leads to understanding.
Consider the role of 'اللوح المحفوظ' as record of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أنه قد ثبت للعبد فعل في الحقيقة، وأنه له مختار، وأنه آثر الأشياء عنده وأحبها، وأن خلق ذلك لم يدفعه إليه، ولم يحمله ولم يضطره إليه . فوجود ذلك ووجود علمه به وخبره عنه وإثباته في اللوح المحفوظ وإيجاب معاودته لوقت فعله وتسميته بما سمّى ـ إذ لم يضطره إلى فعله ولا حمله عليه ـ حسن معه الأمر والنهي والتعذيب والإثابة. ومن أنكر بهذا خلقه فتعلقه بهذا النوع خيال. وحقه أن ينظر في الوجه الذي به يُعرف خلق الأشياء، فإن أمكن تحقيقه فالإنكار بهذا النوع إنما هو جهل بالحكمة. وعلى ذلك كان أول ما جبل عليه . فسيعلم إن خضع للمكرم به إن شاء الله ، وإن لم يمكن تسقط المسألة، ويُفصل الذي عارض به كلُّه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the principle is that it has been established for the servant a real action, and that he has choice, and that he prefers things in his view and loves them, and that the creation of that did not push him to it, nor compel him, nor force him. So the existence of that [action] and the existence of His knowledge of it and His report about it and its inscription in the Preserved Tablet and the obligation of its recurrence at the time of his action and its naming as He named—since He did not force him to his action nor compel him—makes good with it the command and prohibition and punishment and reward. And whoever denies this his creation clings to this type of illusion. And it is right that he consider the aspect by which the creation of things is known; if its verification is possible, then denial of this type is ignorance of wisdom. And upon that was the first thing he was created. So he will know if he submits to the One honored him, God willing, and if it is not possible, the question falls, and everything with which he confronted is set aside. And there is no power but through God.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the Maturidi position on human action and its compatibility with divine decree and command. The author begins by stating the foundational principle: the servant has a real action (fi‘l) in truth (fi al-haqiqa), and he has choice (mukhtar). The servant is the one who prefers (āthara) things and loves them. However, the creation (khalq) of that action did not compel him, nor force him—meaning God's creative power does not negate the servant's genuine choice. The passage then argues that given this reality, divine knowledge, record in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz), and the return of the action at its proper time, together with the fact that God did not force the servant, it is appropriate (hasan) for God to issue commands and prohibitions, and to reward and punish. Whoever denies this aspect of creation (i.e., that human acts are created by God in a way that still allows free choice) is holding an illusion. Such a person should consider how the creation of things is known; if they cannot verify their denial, then their denial is ignorance of wisdom (hikma). The passage concludes by noting that this is the original constitution of humanity (jubila 'alayh), and that if one submits, they will know, otherwise the objection falls away. The phrase 'There is no power but through God' (la quwwata illa billah) closes the argument, acknowledging divine assistance.

Key Terms
فعل
مختار
خلق
أمر
نهي
اللوح المحفوظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that if God creates all actions, humans have no real choice and are forced, making divine command unjust.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's creation of an action does not imply compulsion; the servant has genuine choice and preference, so commands and punishments are just.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the appropriateness of divine command and punishment?
A) God forces all human actions
B) The servant has real action and choice, and God does not compel
C) Human actions are illusions
D) The Preserved Tablet contains only good deeds

Key Takeaway
Maturidi maintains that human beings have genuine choice and action, making divine commands and judgment just, even though God creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever use the belief that everything is from God as an excuse for your sins? How does this passage challenge that excuse, and what would it mean for you to stop hiding behind qadar and take full ownership of your actions?
?
This passage says the servant prefers things and loves them—your heart's choices are real. Where do your deepest loves and preferences lead you? Are you ready to face the reward or punishment for those choices, knowing God did not force you?
?
If you truly believed that no sin of yours was compelled by God, what would change about your repentance today? Would you feel more accountable—and might that accountability draw you closer to Him?

Divine Decree
intermediate
