Refuting Human Power to Oppose God
Maturidi refutes the view that God gives humans power to oppose Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the view that God enables humans to oppose Him as entailing absurd consequences that contradict God's attributes.

Core Idea
Empowering people to oppose God would make God weak and foolish.

Maturidi presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum. If God gave humans the power to break His commands, then humans could make God a liar and ignorant—impossible for the All-Wise. This passage exposes the logical contradictions in that claim and defends God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absurd consequences
Read the first sentence and note the three accusations humans could make against God if they had power to oppose Him: foolishness, ignorance, and breaking promises.
These are the starting point of the reductio.
2
Reflect on the analogy
Consider Maturidi's comparison: even the most foolish person would not enable someone to expose his foolishness. So how could the All-Wise do so? This shows the claimed 'enabling' is impossible.
This highlights the irrationality of the opposing view.
3
Grasp the implications for divine sovereignty
Read the later part about messengers and world governance. Maturidi argues that if humans had such power, God's plan would depend on human cooperation, which would give humans a favor over God—an absurdity.
This demonstrates the threat to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبقاءه قدر على تسفيه الله وتجهيله وخلفه في الوعد ، ومن ذلك وصفه ليس بإله؛ وبمثله نفوا قول الثنوية. مع ما في هذا أمر عجيب أن يكون الله يُقوسى أحدًا على نقض ربوبيته؛ إذ مَلَكَ تصييره كاذبًا وقدر على جعله جاهلاً، وعن وفاء ما وعده عاجزاً. وهذا النوع من الإقدار لا يفعله أسفه السفهاء، فكيف أحكم الحاكمين؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى قول هؤلاء يكون للبشر قدرة نقض تدبير العالم، وللرسل قوة أن لا يظهروا الله حجة في الأرض، وأن يمتنع كل منهم عن الوجه الذي عليه مضى تدبير العالم؛ وهو مبنى على كون أحواله على أيدى البشر وخلق الأرض والسماء ؛ والله تعالى لم يكن له قدرة على خلق تلك الأفعال / والأحوال على أيديهم، ولهم قدرة على أن لا يفعلوا شيئًا من ذلك. فإذا لهم عليه أرفع المنن وأعلى النعم، إذ على القدرة في منع تقديره ونفاذ تدبيره فعلوا الذي به قام تدبيره وتم ملكه وسلطانه على ما دبر وشاء، وذلك أوحش قول وبالله التوفيق.
He preserved power to accuse God of foolishness and ignorance and breaking the promise, and from that, describing Him as not a god; and by the like of it they denied the saying of the dualists. In this is an amazing matter: that God would strengthen someone to break His lordship; because He made him able to make Him a liar and capable of making Him ignorant, and incapable of fulfilling what He promised. This kind of enabling is not done by the most foolish of the foolish, so how about the Most Just of judges? And there is no power except with God. And according to the saying of these, humans have the power to break the arrangement of the world, and the messengers have the power not to manifest God's proof on earth, and that each of them could refrain from the way upon which the arrangement of the world proceeded; and it is based on the fact that His states are by the hands of humans and the creation of the earth and sky; and God Almighty had no power to create those actions and states by their hands, and they have the power not to do any of that. So they have upon Him the highest favors and greatest blessings, since despite having the power to prevent His decree and the execution of His plan, they did that by which His plan was established and His dominion and authority were completed upon what He decreed and willed. That is the ugliest saying. And by God is success.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that God gives humans the ability to oppose His will. Maturidi begins by stating the absurd consequences: such an endowment would allow humans to accuse God of foolishness and ignorance and to make Him break His promises, which would mean He is not truly God. He then points out that even the most foolish person would not enable someone to expose his own foolishness, so how could the All-Wise do so? He then elaborates: if humans had such power, they could thwart God's plan for the world, and messengers could refuse to convey divine proofs. This would imply that God's dominion depends on human cooperation, and humans would have a favor upon God for choosing to follow His plan. This is an abominable position.

Key Terms
قدرة
تسفيه
تجهيل
خلف الوعد
إله
ربوبية
ثنوية
إقدار
منن
تدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming human free will means humans have independent power to oppose God's will.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such power would allow humans to make God a liar and ignorant, which is impossible for God. True human agency is created by God and does not give humans independent power to thwart His will.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would be the consequence if God gave humans power to oppose Him?
A) God would be showing His mercy.
B) God would be enabling them to make Him a liar and ignorant.
C) Humans would be able to perform miracles.
D) God's power would be increased.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that affirming human power to oppose God leads to attributing weakness and ignorance to God, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial you begged God to remove, but it remained, do you secretly harbor resentment as if God 'broke a promise'? This lesson says such a thought accuses God of ignorance. Will you confess that feeling to God and repent of it today?
?
You claim to believe nothing happens outside God's will. But when you sin, do you internally boast that you 'chose' to defy Him, as if you overpowered His command? How would your repentance change if you truly saw your sin as only occurring by His permission?
?
The most foolish person would not enable another to expose his foolishness. God is the Most Wise. Look at your life: where have you been acting as if you can frustrate God's plan? What would it cost you to surrender that illusion right now?

The Language of Excuse: Power with the Act
Maturidi argues that people's everyday statements imply power concurrent with the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses everyday language to argue that human power is concurrent with actions.

Core Idea
Your own excuses reveal that power is tied to the action.

Maturidi makes a clever observation: when people say 'I cannot do this because...', they implicitly acknowledge that they have a certain power relevant to that specific act. This everyday language, he argues, points to a theological truth about the nature of human power (qudra). Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the examples Maturidi uses: 'I am not able because of my occupation' and 'I cannot move this.'
2
Focus on the logical structure
Identify the steps: (1) Observable fact: people say such things. (2) Theological premise: God would not let them utter a falsehood. (3) Conclusion: Their words imply a power that is present with the act, not separate.
3
Consider the implication for qadar
Think about why this matters: it supports the view that human power is not a pre-existing capacity but is created by God at the time of the act (al-qudra al-mu'aththira).
This contrasts with views that posit a power independent of the act.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to see if you have grasped the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم وجود هذا القول ظاهر في الخلق : لا أقدر لشغلى بكذا»، أو «لا أستطيع بنقل هذا على. ولم يجز أن يكون الله ينطق ألسن الخلق على غير تمانع منهم بما هو كذب في الحقيقة، وهم يعلمون أن استطاعة الأسباب والأحوال؛ فثبت أن وراء ذلك عندهم قدرة معهم يذكرونها مع الاعتذار في الأفعال لا في الجمل التي تُرجع الأوهام إلى الأحوال. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the existence of this saying is apparent among people: 'I am not able because of my occupation with such-and-such,' or 'I cannot move this.' And it is not permissible that God would make the tongues of people speak, without opposition from them, what is actually false, while they know the ability (istita'a) of causes and states; so it is established that behind that they have a power with them which they mention as an excuse in actions, not in the sentences that revert the illusions to states. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is an argument from common linguistic usage. Maturidi points out that people often excuse themselves by saying 'I cannot do this due to my occupation' or 'I am unable to move this.' In saying so, they implicitly recognize that they have a certain power that is tied to the particular action at hand. Since God would not let people speak falsehood without correction, this indicates that the power (qudra) they refer to is concurrent with the act, not a separate power existing before it. The 'excuse' is about the action, not a general state.

Everyday language of excuse reveals that human power is concurrent with the action, not a pre-existing capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Māturīdī argues that everyday speech is truthful because God wouldn't let falsehood persist unchecked. When you use phrases like 'I am unable,' are you speaking truthfully? Or do you sometimes use excuses to avoid responsibility? Reflect on a specific instance where your excuse masked a lack of trust in Allah's provision of concurrent power. What will you do differently the next time you feel incapable?
?

But have you truly internalized that your ability to act in this very moment—to read, to think, to decide—is from Allah? Consider a task you have been procrastinating. What would change if you began it with the certainty that Allah grants the power to do it right now? Will you start today?

Maturidi's Proof that Power is Concurrent with the Act
Maturidi argues that power is an accident, and since accidents do not endure, it cannot precede the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that human power (quwwa) is an accident and therefore must coincide with the act, not precede it.

Core Idea
Power is an accident; accidents do not endure, so power must arise with the act.

This lesson unpacks a key argument from Imam Maturidi on the nature of human ability (istita'a). He begins by identifying what power is—an accident—and then draws out the logical consequences of that classification. The argument is foundational for the Maturidi position that ability is not a pre-existing entity but rather created concurrently with the action. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the compact argumentative style.
The passage begins with a heading in brackets.
2
Identify the key premise: power is an accident
Maturidi states that power is not part of the body, therefore it is an accident (‘arad). This classification is crucial.
3
Understand why accidents do not endure
Accidents cannot endure because they cannot subsist by themselves; they require a substance and cannot receive other states (aghyar). So power, as an accident, cannot persist.
The logic is that if power could endure, it would need an enduring quality in something else, which is impossible.
4
Follow the conclusion: power must be with the act
Since power cannot endure before the act, it must come into existence at the moment of the act. Otherwise, acts would be corrupted by prior causes.
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except with God,' affirming divine causation.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
This argument negates the idea that humans have a pre-existing power to act independently. Power is created by God concurrently with the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الاستطاعة قبل الفعل أم معه ؟] والنظر في ذلك أن القوة إذ ليست هي من أجزاء الجسم فهي عرض في الحقيقة. والأعراض لا تبقى، إذ لا يجوز بقاء ما يحتمل الفناء إلا ببقاء هو غيره ؛ والعرض لا يقبل الأغيار بما لا قيام له بذاته، ومحال بقاء الشيء ببقاء في غيره، فبطل البقاء. ثم فساد حقيقة الأفعال بأسباب متقدمة [ يتحقق ] إذا لم تكن هي وقت الفعل، فمثله قوة الفعل، فيلزم القول بالكون مع الفعل. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
[Ability before the act or with it?] And the consideration in that is that power, since it is not part of the body, is in reality an accident. And accidents do not endure, because it is not permissible for something that admits passing away to endure except by an enduring other than itself; and an accident does not receive other states (aghyar) because it has no subsistence by itself. And it is impossible for a thing to endure by an enduring in something else, so endurance is invalid. Then the corruption of the reality of actions by prior causes is realized if they are not at the time of the action; similarly the power of the action, so the statement that it is with the act follows. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof that ability (istita'a) is concurrent with the act. Maturidi first notes that power (quwwa) is not a bodily part but an accident (‘arad). Accidents, by their nature, do not endure (tabqa). If a power could endure, it would require an enduring substance, but accidents have no self-subsistence. Therefore, power cannot exist before the act and then persist until the act occurs. Thus, power must come into existence at the moment of the act. This is a foundational argument for the Ash‘ari-Maturidi position that ability does not precede the act.

Key Terms
استطاعة
قوة
عرض
بقاء
فناء
أغيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think human power exists as a separate entity prior to the act, enabling the action independently.
Correction: Maturidi proves that power is an accident which cannot endure, so it cannot exist before the act; it must arise with the act, and all power is from God.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that power cannot exist before the act?
A) Because power is a substance.
B) Because power is an accident and accidents do not endure.
C) Because the act creates the power.
D) Because power is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi proves that human power is an accident and must arise simultaneously with the act, not before it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you assume you have stored up power or ability that you can use later, rather than recognizing that it is granted only at the moment? How will you change your approach to that area today before the opportunity passes?

Reflect on a time you delayed an action because you thought you could build up strength beforehand. Does this lesson reveal a flaw in that thinking? What will you do differently starting now?
?
The source ends with 'And there is no power except with God.' In what specific situation do you need to admit that your own ability is nothing without God's concurrent granting of power? How will you practically acknowledge that in your next decision?

Power Is Simultaneous with Action: Maturidi's Proof
Maturidi argues that prior power leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that power cannot exist before the act because it would then be for something impossible, establishing that power is simultaneous with action.

Core Idea
Prior power contradicts later incapacity, proving power is simultaneous with the act.

This lesson presents Maturidi's second proof that divine power (or human power) must be simultaneous with the action it produces. If power were prior, a later incapacity would make that power aim at an impossibility—a logical contradiction. This concise argument reinforces the Ash'ari and Maturidi position that power is created at the moment of action.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then... contradictory.'
Pay attention to key terms: قوة, قدرة, عجز, متناقض.
2
Identify the premise and the contradiction
The passage states two premises: power is for actions, and incapacity can occur after having power. Then it draws the conclusion: if power were prior to the act, it would be for something one is incapable of—a contradiction.
The contradiction is that power cannot be for an impossible act.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the scenario leads to a contradiction, the assumption that power precedes the act is false. Therefore, power must be simultaneous with the act.
The final phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' emphasizes that all power is from God.
4
Apply the reasoning
Consider an example: if someone had the power to stand now, but later becomes paralyzed, then their earlier power would have been for something impossible (standing after paralysis). This shows power cannot precede the act.
This argument is used in kalam to defend that power is created at the moment of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن القوة إذ هي للأفعال وجائز حدوث العجز بعد الوصف بالقدرة، فلو كانت القدرة للفعل بعده لكانت لما هو عنه عاجز، وذلك متناقض فاسد. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, since power is for actions and it is possible for incapacity to occur after being described with power, if power were for the act after it, it would be for that which one is incapable of, and that is contradictory and invalid. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This is a second proof. Maturidi argues that if power existed before the act, there is a scenario where the person later becomes incapable (e.g., due to illness or death). The prior power would then have been for an act that is no longer possible, which is contradictory. Power cannot be for an impossible act. Therefore, power must be simultaneous with the act.

Key Terms
قوة
قدرة
عجز
فعل
متناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that power can exist separately from the act and still be valid, like a potentiality that remains even if the act is never done.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if power were prior, it could become directed at an impossible act, which is contradictory. Power must be concurrent with the act it produces.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, what contradiction arises if power existed before the act?
A) Power would cause incapacity.
B) If incapacity occurs later, the prior power would be for something impossible.
C) Power would be independent of God.
D) Power would be unknowable.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's second proof establishes that power cannot precede its act; it must be simultaneous, otherwise it would aim at an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a moment when you suddenly lost the capacity to do something you intended. How does the impossibility of prior power make you reconsider your procrastination?
?
If all your power is only present at the moment of action, what does that imply about the sincerity of your intentions when you delay obedience? Will you commit to acting as soon as you have the ability?

Prior Power Entails Independence from God
Maturidi argues that power before the act would make humans independent of God before it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third argument against prior human power, which shows that such power would contradict the creature's perpetual need for God.

Core Idea
Prior power would make humans independent of God before the act—impossible by revelation and reason.

Maturidi continues his refutation of the idea that human power exists before the act. In this compact argument, he demonstrates that if power preceded the action, humans would be independent of God at that moment. But the Qur'an teaches that all creatures are always needy of God, who is the Self-Sufficient. Therefore, prior power is impossible. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument step by step.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' – it's a key Qur'anic echo.
2
Identify the premise and consequence
Maturidi states: if powers existed before actions, then a person would be independent of God before the act. This is the core logical conditional.
Consider what 'independent' means in this context: no need for God's creation of power at the time of action.
3
Understand the contradiction
The consequence contradicts the Qur'anic statement that God made all creatures needy of Him and He is the Self-Sufficient. So prior power is impermissible.
Also note the secondary point: even if power does not endure, prior power still implies independence before existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن القوى لو كانت لأحوال ترد لكان بها يستغنى عن الله في جميع الأفعال قبل وجود الأفعال، والله جل ثناؤه صيّر الخلق جميعًا فقراء إليه وهو الغنى الحميد؛ لم يجز أن يقع لهم الغنى عنه بأحق ما لزمت الحاجة . والأصل أنها إذ كانت لا تبقى تزول حاجة البقاء، والفعل ليس بموجود، فيصير غنيا عن الله قبل كونه، وذلك قبيح في السمع. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, if powers were for states that come, by them one would be independent of God in all actions before the existence of actions, while God, exalted be His praise, made all creatures needful of Him, and He is the Praiseworthy Self-Sufficient. It is not permissible for them to become independent of Him at the time when need is most binding. And the principle is that since they do not endure, the need for endurance disappears, and the action is not existent, so one becomes independent of God before its coming into existence, and that is repugnant in the revealed law. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This is a third argument against prior power. Maturidi notes that if power existed before the act, a person would be independent of God at that moment. But God says all creatures are always in need of Him. Furthermore, even if power does not endure, the presence of a prior power would mean the person has no need for God to create the power at the time of the act. This contradicts the universal dependence on God and is repugnant to the Shari‘a.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that human power could exist before the act without making humans independent of God, perhaps because the power itself is created by God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any prior power, even if created, would mean that at that moment the human does not need God to create power at the time of the act—hence independence from God before the act, which contradicts the perpetual need of creation.

Checkpoint

A) Humans would be independent of God before the act.
B) Humans would be more responsible for their actions.
C) God would be independent of humans.
D) Actions would occur without divine will.

Key Takeaway
Prior human power is impossible because it would imply independence from God before the act, contradicting the creature's essential need for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Does this belief motivate you to beg Him for His help? Or do you rely on your own willpower? What step will you take today to practice 'lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh' in that struggle?

Power Known Only Through the Act
Maturidi argues that power is only known through the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that power cannot be known or exist prior to the act.

Core Idea
Power's reality is only known through the act it produces.

Maturidi presents a subtle epistemological argument about power (qudra). He asserts that we have no direct knowledge of what power is; we only understand it through the act it produces. This leads to a conclusion about the timing of power relative to the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let the author's train of thought settle before analysing it.
Notice the key term 'شهد' (witnessed) – it indicates direct evidence.
2
Identify the main claim
Maturidi opens by stating that power is not known by its essence or by a limit. Pinpoint this negative claim – it sets up the whole argument.
The phrase 'لا تُعلَم لذاتها' is central.
3
Follow the reasoning to the conclusion
Maturidi reasons: power is only known through the act; the act is not existent before power; therefore power is only witnessed at the time of the act, not before. Trace this logical chain.
The conclusion 'شهد لها وقت كونه لا قبله' directly addresses the timing of power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن القوة ليست تُعلَم لذاتها ولا لها حد يعلم حقيقتها، سوى ما جعلها الله على حقيقة كونها من الفعل، والفعل ليس بموجود قبل كونها، وبها وجوده؛ ثبت أنه شهد لها وقت كونه لا قبله. والله الموفق.
And also, power is not known by its essence nor does it have a limit by which its reality is known, except what God made it upon the reality of its being from the act; and the act is not existent before its coming into being, and by it is its existence; so it is established that it is witnessed at the time of its coming into being, not before it. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This is an epistemological argument. Maturidi states that we have no independent knowledge of what power is apart from its relation to the act. The only way we understand power is through the act it produces. Since the act does not exist before the power, power cannot be known or said to exist prior to the act. Therefore, power must arise simultaneously with the act.

Key Terms
قوة
علم
حقيقة
فعل
شهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is an eternal attribute that exists before any action.
Correction: Maturidi argues that power is only known through the act and that the act does not exist before power, so power cannot precede the act. This challenges the notion of a pre-existing, independent power.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say power cannot be known before the act?
A) Because power is a hidden attribute.
B) Because power's reality is only known through the act, and the act does not exist before power.
C) Because power is not an accident.
D) Because power is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Power's reality is inseparable from the act; it cannot be known or exist prior to the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan to commit a sin, you assume you already have the power to do it. But this lesson says power is only known at the moment of the act. How does this change your understanding of your own ability to sin, and your need for Allah's protection in that split second?
?
Think of a good deed you keep putting off. Are you assuming that your power to do it will still be there tomorrow? What would it mean to truly rely on Allah for that power only when you actually begin the act?
?
If power is not a reservoir you possess, but something that arises with the act, then what is the state of your heart before you act? Are you filled with self-sufficiency or humble dependence on the One who grants power in that very moment?

Fifth Proof: The Capable Person Must Act
Maturidi argues that a capable person necessarily acts, so power and act coexist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that a truly capable person necessarily acts, establishing the coexistence of power and action.

Core Idea
A capable person must act; power and act are inseparable.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents the fifth proof for the simultaneity of power and act. He argues that we never find a genuinely capable person who does not act, just as we never find an incapable person acting. Therefore, affirming power while denying its corresponding act is rationally impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument and addresses potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Start by reading the Arabic and its translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the parallel structure Maturidi uses.
2
Identify the core claim
Identify Maturidi's central claim: there is no capable person who is not acting.
This is the premise.
3
Understand the objection and response
Consider the possible counterexample of death or sleep. Maturidi argues death is incapacity, not power.
This strengthens the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that power and act must coexist, as denying one while affirming the other is contradictory.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لا يوجد قادر غير فاعل ألبتة كما لا يوجد عاجز فاعلاً، لم يجز القضاء بالقدرة ونفي الفعل، كما لا يجوز العجز ووجوده؛ إذ هما جميعا في الخروج عن الموجود واحد؛ مع القول بالبعد عن ذلك من طريق العقل، من حيث تضاد المعنى في الحقيقة. وليس بالموت ونحوه معتبر، لأن الموت عجز في الجملة وليست الحياة بقدرة في الجملة، ولما يجوز وجود الحياة أوقاتاً لا فعل معها، ولا يجوز وجود قادر وقتين لا فعل له. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, there is absolutely no capable person who is not acting, just as there is no incapable person acting. It is not permissible to judge power with the negation of the act, just as it is not permissible to judge incapacity with its existence; since both are equal in exiting from the existent; along with the statement of remoteness from that by way of reason, in terms of the contradiction in reality. And death and the like are not considered, because death is incapacity in general, and life is not power in general, and it is not permissible for life to exist at times with no act, and it is not permissible for a capable person to exist for two times with no act. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This final proof relies on the observable correlation between capability and action. Maturidi states that we never find a truly capable person who does not act, nor an incapable person who does act. Therefore, if we assert power, we must also assert the act. The counterexample of death does not apply because death is a complete absence of ability, not a power. Similarly, life does not necessarily entail power, but if someone is alive and capable, they must act. Thus, power and act are inseparable.

Key Terms
قادر
فاعل
عاجز
فعل
عجز
موت
حياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person can be capable but not act, e.g., while sleeping or dead.
Correction: Maturidi argues that death is complete incapacity, not power. Sleep is temporary, but if truly capable, the person would act when conditions are present. The proof rests on the definition of capability: it entails action.

Checkpoint
What is the final argument Maturidi gives for the simultaneity of power and act?
A) There is no capable person who is not acting.
B) The Qur'an explicitly states it.
C) The companions agreed upon it.
D) Reason cannot grasp it.

Key Takeaway
A truly capable person necessarily acts; therefore, power and act are inseparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you claim to be capable but consistently delay or avoid action? What does this lesson reveal about the honesty of your claim?
?
The passage states that a capable person cannot exist for two moments without acting. When you waste time or procrastinate, are you truly incapable, or are you denying the very power God has given you? How will you respond starting today?
?
The proof ends with 'There is no power except with God.' When you accomplish something, do you instinctively attribute the success to your own ability? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that your power is borrowed from Him?

Power Suitable for Both Obedience and Disobedience
Maturidi presents the first view on power and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first view that power is suitable for both obedience and disobedience, its basis in causality, and its implication for the doctrine of taklif ma la yutaq.

Core Idea
If power only served one opposite, commands and prohibitions would be meaningless.

This lesson focuses on the first of two positions concerning human power (qudra). The view attributed to Abu Hanifa and many others holds that the same power can produce either obedience or disobedience. This is necessary to maintain that divine commands and prohibitions are within human capacity. The passage also notes that the Mu'tazila affirm this view, which leads them to accept the problematic notion of obligating what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the opening lines to see that the passage presents a disagreement on whether power for obedience is suitable for disobedience. Note the two groups mentioned.
The first view is introduced first.
2
Understand the basis in causality
Notice how the author argues that just as every cause is suitable for something and its opposite, so power must be suitable for both opposites. This is a causal principle.
The phrase 'كل سبب من أسباب الفعل تصلح للشيء وضده' is key.
3
Grasp the implication for commands and prohibitions
If power were only for one thing, the opposite act would be impossible, making commands or prohibitions meaningless. This leads to the concept of taklif ma la yutaq.
The author suggests the Mu'tazila are forced into this problematic position.
4
Reflect on the natural vs. voluntary distinction
Understand the principle: if power were not suitable for both, actions would occur by nature, not by choice. This would undermine human responsibility.
The last part of the passage for this lesson emphasizes choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
القول في صلاحية القدرة للضدين وتكليف ما لا يطاق
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم اختلف أهل هذا القول في قوة الطاعة أهي تصلح للمعصية أم لا؟ قال جماعة: هي تصلح للأمرين جميعا، وهو قول أبي حنيفة وجماعة. وهذا القول أثبته جميع أهل الاعتزال عند التأمل، ويحقق عليهم القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق، وذلك سببهم في القول بتقدم القوة. والله الموفق. وأصل هذا أنه لما كان كل سبب من أسباب الفعل تصلح للشيء وضده فكذلك القدرة - مع ما في نفي أن تصلح للأمرين فوت القدرة على فعل ضد الذي جاء به، وقد يؤمر به وينهى عنه في وقته - فيلزم القول بالقدرة على الشيء وضده، ليكون الأمر والنهي على الوسع والقوة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الأصل أن كل شيء يصلح لشيء ولا يصلح لضده يكون الذي به بالطبع لا بالاختيار، ولو كانت القوة لا تصلح لهما لكان ما كان يقع بالطبع لا بالاختيار. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقال جماعة منهم: قوة الطاعة هي غير قوة المعصية، منهم الحسين وغيره. وهم يذهبون إلى أن قوة الطاعة التوفيق والعصمة، وقوة المعصية الخذلان والترك على ما يختار. ودليل ذلك وجود سؤال المعونة والعصمة على الإحاطة أن ليس معهما زيغ، والتوفيق على الإحاطة أن معه الإصابة. وكذلك القول الظاهر بـ«اللهم قوتي على طاعتك وأعني عليها»، وبالتعوذ من الخذلان والإزاغة. ثبت أن لو كان يكون بكل واحد منهما ما يكون بالآخر لم يكن الذي يسأل بالسؤال أحق من الذي يتعوذ منه. ولو كان يكون بالعصمة زيغ لم يكن.
Issue: The statement on the suitability of power for opposites and the imposition of what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq). The jurist Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the people of this view differed concerning the power for obedience: is it suitable for disobedience or not? A group said: It is suitable for both matters, and that is the saying of Abu Hanifa and a group. And this saying is affirmed by all the Mu'tazila upon reflection, and the saying of imposing what cannot be borne is verified against them, and that is the reason for their saying that power precedes. And God is the grantor of success. And the basis of this is: since every cause of an act is suitable for the thing and its opposite, so too power—with what is in the negation that it is suitable for both matters, [i.e.] the loss of power over the opposite of that which one has come with, and one may be commanded and prohibited from it at its time—so it necessitates the saying that one has power over the thing and its opposite, so that the command and prohibition are within capacity and power. And there is no power except with God. Then the principle is: everything that is suitable for a thing and not suitable for its opposite is that by which [the act] occurs by nature not by choice. And if power were not suitable for both, then what occurs would occur by nature not by choice. And there is no power except with God. And a group among them said: The power for obedience is other than the power for disobedience, among them al-Husayn and others. And they hold that the power for obedience is tawfiq (divine facilitation) and `isma (preservation), and the power for disobedience is khidhlan (abandonment) and tark (letting go) according to what is chosen. And the evidence for that is the existence of the request for aid (ma`una) and preservation (isma) with the comprehension that there is no deviation with them, and tawfiq with the comprehension that with it is correctness. And likewise the apparent saying: 'O God, strengthen me for Your obedience and help me upon it,' and seeking refuge from abandonment and deviation. It is established that if each of them could produce what the other produces, then the one who asks [for guidance] would not be more entitled than the one who seeks refuge [from deviation]. And if preservation could have deviation, it would not be...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion on the nature of human power (qudra) in relation to obedience and disobedience. The author presents two positions. The first, attributed to Abu Hanifa and many others, holds that power is suitable for both opposite actions (obedience and disobedience). This is necessary to account for both command and prohibition: the same power can do either. If power only served one, the opposite act would be impossible, making the prohibition or command meaningless (taklif ma la yutaq). The Mu'tazila also held this view, but the author notes that it forces them to accept the notion of taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of what cannot be borne), which is problematic. The second view, from al-Husayn al-Najjar and others, distinguishes between power for obedience (which is divine facilitation and preservation) and power for disobedience (which is abandonment). They argue from the evidence of prayer: people ask God for strength and preservation in obedience and seek refuge from abandonment. If the same power could produce both, the prayer would be meaningless because the same power could be used for disobedience. Therefore, the two powers are distinct. The passage ends with a logical argument: if preservation (isma) could include deviation, then it would not truly be preservation.

القدرة
الطاعة
المعصية
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think power can only produce one type of action, making opposite commands impossible.
Correction: The first view shows that power must be suitable for both opposites to maintain human responsibility and the meaningfulness of divine commands.

Checkpoint
According to the first view (Abu Hanifa), power is:
A) Only for obedience.
B) Only for disobedience.
C) Suitable for both obedience and disobedience.
D) Not related to action.

Key Takeaway
Power must be suitable for both obedience and disobedience to make commands and prohibitions meaningful and actions voluntary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever tell yourself 'I had no choice' or 'It was beyond my control'? How does this lesson's assertion that power covers both obedience and disobedience confront that excuse? Today, when tempted, will you still claim powerlessness?
?
If you truly believed that your power from God is equally suited to obeying and disobeying, would your daily decisions look any different? Where are you currently acting as if you have no choice but to sin? What will you change today to reflect that you do have the power—and therefore the responsibility—to choose obedience?
?
This lesson says that if power were not suitable for both opposites, your actions would be by nature, not by choice. Is there an area of your life where you have been living as if your habits are 'just the way you are'—as if by nature? How does this truth call you to take full ownership of your choices starting now?

Distinct Powers for Obedience and Disobedience
Maturidi presents the second view distinguishing powers for obedience and disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view that distinguishes power for obedience as divine facilitation and preservation, and power for disobedience as abandonment, supported by evidence from supplications.

Core Idea
Divine facilitation (tawfiq) enables obedience; abandonment (khidhlan) leaves one to disobedience.

This lesson examines the second position, attributed to al-Husayn al-Najjar and others, which holds that the power for obedience is not the same as the power for disobedience. Obedience is enabled by tawfiq (divine facilitation) and 'isma (preservation), while disobedience results from khidhlan (abandonment) and tark (letting go). The proponents argue from common prayers: people ask God for strength to obey and seek refuge from abandonment, implying these are distinct powers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second view
Find the sentence introducing the second group and their claim that power for obedience is different from power for disobedience.
The names al-Husayn and others are mentioned.
2
Understand the terms tawfiq and 'isma
Learn that tawfiq is divine facilitation that leads to correct action, and 'isma is preservation from error. They are the powers for obedience.
Khidhlan and tark are the opposite for disobedience.
3
Analyze the evidence from supplication
Examine the prayer 'O God, strengthen me for Your obedience' and seeking refuge from abandonment. These show that obedience requires a special grace.
The logic: if the same power could produce both, the prayer would be meaningless.
4
Consider the concluding argument
Reflect on the last sentence: if preservation ('isma) could include deviation, it would not be true preservation. This reinforces the distinctness.
The passage ends abruptly; the argument implies that true preservation excludes deviation.

The Unity of Tawfiq and 'Isma
Maturidi argues for the unity of tawfiq and 'isma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves that tawfiq (divine enabling) and 'isma (preservation) are essentially one and the same divine assistance for faith.

Core Idea
Tawfiq and 'isma are one: they are assistance to faith.

This lesson focuses on a key argument from Imam al-Maturidi that two central concepts in Islamic theology—tawfiq and 'isma—are not separate gifts but are in reality one and the same. Maturidi uses a simple observation: people ask for them just as they ask for assistance and strengthening, and no one attributes these qualities to a disbeliever. This points to their essential unity as assistance for faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two separate arguments Maturidi presents to prove the unity of tawfiq and 'isma.
The passage begins with a note about the heart's tranquility, which sets the stage for the first conclusion about distinct powers.
2
Identify the first argument: asking analogy
Maturidi observes that people ask for 'isma and tawfiq just as they ask for assistance and strengthening. This similarity implies that the pair are actually one in reality.
Ask yourself: why would two distinct gifts be asked for using the same pattern?
3
Identify the second argument: believer/disbeliever distinction
No one says a disbeliever is muwaffaq (successful) or ma'sum (protected) for belief; similarly, no one denies this for a believer. This shows that tawfiq and 'isma are essentially assistance to faith, and their opposite is khidhlan (abandonment).
This argument relies on common linguistic usage and consensus.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Together, these two arguments establish that tawfiq and 'isma are one reality: divine assistance to stay on the path of faith.
Reflect on why this unity matters for understanding divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تبقى يطمئن القلب عند الوجود . فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما في الحقيقة واحد. وأيضا إنه لا أحد يطلق القول في الكافر أنه موفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن؛ ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان، والآخر الخذلان وأيضاً إن القوة إذ هي وقتين لا يصلح بها الفعلان ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد؛ ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالهما. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إن القوة لا يجوز وجودها إلا وثمة اضطرار ، كالنار في التحريق والثلج في التبريد، إنه يقع به الذي له طبع بالاضطرار؛ وذلك كالولاية مع الإيمان، والعداوة مع الكفران، سببهما مختلف على اختلافهما، فمثله أمر القوة على الأمرين. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
It does not remain; the heart is assured at existence. So it is established that the power of each type of that is different from the power of the other type, and it is established by what is asked of al-ʿiṣma (preservation) and al-tawfīq (divine enabling) just as one asks for al-muʿāwana (assistance) and al-taqwiya (strengthening) that they are in reality one. And also that no one applies the statement to the disbeliever that he is muwaffaq (divinely enabled) for belief and maʿṣūm (preserved) from disbelief, nor does anyone refrain from it regarding the believer; it is established that the meaning of that is assistance to faith, and the other is khidhlān (abandonment). And also that when power exists in two moments, the two acts are not accomplished by it, and there is no way to combine two opposing acts in one moment; it is established that it is a power for one of them, not for both, and that which is for both would remain for its possibility for both. And there is no power except by Allah. And also that power cannot exist except with necessity (iḍṭirār), like fire in burning and snow in cooling, it occurs by that which has a nature necessarily; and that is like al-walāya (loyalty) with faith, and al-ʿadāwa (enmity) with disbelief, their causes are different according to their difference, so the matter of power over the two matters is similar. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a proof that power (quwwa) for different types of acts is distinct, and that divine gifts like tawfiq (enabling) and 'isma (preservation) are essentially one. Maturidi begins by stating that the heart's tranquility at existence establishes that power for each type of act is different. He argues that when people ask for 'isma and tawfiq just as they ask for mu'awana (assistance) and taqwiya (strengthening), it shows they are one in reality. He supports this by noting that no one says a disbeliever has tawfiq for belief or 'isma from disbelief, and similarly for a believer—thus tawfiq is assistance to faith, and its opposite is khidhlan (abandonment). Then he argues that if power existed in two moments, it could not produce two opposing acts simultaneously, so it must be power for one act, not both. Finally, he likens power to natural forces like fire and snow that necessitate their effects by nature, concluding that the power for obedience and disobedience is distinct. The repeated phrase 'wa lā quwwata illā billāh' (and there is no power except by Allah) reinforces that all power is from Allah.

تقوية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think tawfiq and 'isma are separate divine gifts that can be given independently.
Correction: Maturidi proves they are in reality one: both are assistance to faith, and their opposite is divine abandonment.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the fact that people ask for tawfiq and 'isma just as they ask for mu'awana and taqwiya indicate?
A) That these are distinct gifts
B) That they are in reality one
C) That they are unnecessary for believers
D) That they only apply to disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Tawfiq and 'isma are essentially one; they are divine assistance for faith, and their opposite is khidhlan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that your faith is due to your own strength or choices? This lesson teaches that even preservation from disbelief (''isma) is purely divine assistance. Does pride in your faith linger in your heart? How will you cultivate gratitude to Allah today for keeping you in the fold of iman?
?
If tawfiq and ''isma are one and entirely from Allah, then your faith depends completely on His continuous support. Are you living as if this is true? Or does your life reflect an attitude that you are the one holding onto faith? What specific practice can you adopt to remind yourself of your absolute dependence on Him?
?
The opposite of this divine assistance is khidhlan (abandonment). Are there any actions or habits in your life that risk invoking khidhlan? What would it mean for you to be left to your own devices? Ask Allah to protect you from this and to grant you constant tawfiq.

Distinct Powers for Opposing Acts
Maturidi proves the distinctness of powers for opposing acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the power (quwwa) for each act is distinct and cannot be the same power for two opposing acts.

Core Idea
One power cannot produce two opposing acts; each act has its own power.

This lesson continues Maturidi's argument on divine power. He presents two proofs that the power for obedience and the power for disobedience must be distinct. The first proof is temporal: a single power existing at two different moments cannot accomplish two opposite acts simultaneously. The second proof is analogical: power operates by necessity, like fire burning or snow cooling, and since loyalty and enmity are opposite, their powers must be different. The lesson emphasizes that all power is from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two distinct proofs Maturidi offers for the distinctness of powers.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'wa la quwwata illa billah' which reinforces the source of all power.
2
Understand the temporal argument
Maturidi argues that if power existed in two moments, it could not produce two different acts in one moment; opposing acts cannot be combined. Therefore, the power must be for one act, not both.
Think about why this argument rules out a single power for both obedience and disobedience.
3
Understand the necessity argument
Power, like natural forces, necessitates its effect. Fire burns by nature, snow cools. Similarly, loyalty (walaya) accompanies faith and enmity (adawa) accompanies disbelief. Since their causes are different, the powers are different.
This argument uses the idea that power necessarily brings about its proper act.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments show that the power for faith and the power for disbelief are distinct. All power ultimately comes from Allah.
Consider the implications for human responsibility and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تبقى يطمئن القلب عند الوجود . فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما في الحقيقة واحد. وأيضا إنه لا أحد يطلق القول في الكافر أنه موفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن؛ ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان، والآخر الخذلان وأيضاً إن القوة إذ هي وقتين لا يصلح بها الفعلان ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد؛ ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالهما. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إن القوة لا يجوز وجودها إلا وثمة اضطرار ، كالنار في التحريق والثلج في التبريد، إنه يقع به الذي له طبع بالاضطرار؛ وذلك كالولاية مع الإيمان، والعداوة مع الكفران، سببهما مختلف على اختلافهما، فمثله أمر القوة على الأمرين. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
It does not remain; the heart is assured at existence. So it is established that the power of each type of that is different from the power of the other type, and it is established by what is asked of al-ʿiṣma (preservation) and al-tawfīq (divine enabling) just as one asks for al-muʿāwana (assistance) and al-taqwiya (strengthening) that they are in reality one. And also that no one applies the statement to the disbeliever that he is muwaffaq (divinely enabled) for belief and maʿṣūm (preserved) from disbelief, nor does anyone refrain from it regarding the believer; it is established that the meaning of that is assistance to faith, and the other is khidhlān (abandonment). And also that when power exists in two moments, the two acts are not accomplished by it, and there is no way to combine two opposing acts in one moment; it is established that it is a power for one of them, not for both, and that which is for both would remain for its possibility for both. And there is no power except by Allah. And also that power cannot exist except with necessity (iḍṭirār), like fire in burning and snow in cooling, it occurs by that which has a nature necessarily; and that is like al-walāya (loyalty) with faith, and al-ʿadāwa (enmity) with disbelief, their causes are different according to their difference, so the matter of power over the two matters is similar. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a proof that power (quwwa) for different types of acts is distinct, and that divine gifts like tawfiq (enabling) and 'isma (preservation) are essentially one. Maturidi begins by stating that the heart's tranquility at existence establishes that power for each type of act is different. He argues that when people ask for 'isma and tawfiq just as they ask for mu'awana (assistance) and taqwiya (strengthening), it shows they are one in reality. He supports this by noting that no one says a disbeliever has tawfiq for belief or 'isma from disbelief, and similarly for a believer—thus tawfiq is assistance to faith, and its opposite is khidhlan (abandonment). Then he argues that if power existed in two moments, it could not produce two opposing acts simultaneously, so it must be power for one act, not both. Finally, he likens power to natural forces like fire and snow that necessitate their effects by nature, concluding that the power for obedience and disobedience is distinct. The repeated phrase 'wa lā quwwata illā billāh' (and there is no power except by Allah) reinforces that all power is from Allah.

Correction: Maturidi shows that opposing acts cannot be accomplished by the same power because they require different causal connections.

?

How does that change your view of your own responsibility? Will you commit to thanking Allah for the power to obey, and repenting for the power to disobey as a separate trial from Him?
?

Are your loyalties and enmities truly based on faith and disbelief, or on worldly attachments? What is one relationship you need to realign in light of this distinction?
?
Maturidi argues that no power exists except by Allah. In your daily life, do you ever feel self-sufficient, as if your abilities are your own? Refutation of Mu'tazilite View on Qudra and Implied Coercion
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazilite position that qudra does not last, showing it implies compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite view on qudra reduces actions to compulsion and undermines moral responsibility.

Core Idea
If power is not present at action, then action is compelled, not free.

Maturidi begins by acknowledging that the Mu'tazilite position sounds plausible, but upon analysis it leads to logical absurdities. He argues that denying the persistence of qudra at the moment of action effectively makes the agent powerless and compelled, which contradicts the basis for moral categories like friendship and enmity with Allah. This passage shows how kalam refutes flawed premises by tracing their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Maturidi starts by conceding the Mu'tazilite view sounds pleasant before refuting it.
The passage is a dense logical argument; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find where Maturidi states the Mu'tazilite position: that qudra does not last for two moments and is not present at the time of the act.
This is the premise that leads to compulsion.
3
Follow the deduction to compulsion
Maturidi argues that if power is absent at the time of action, the act occurs by necessity (idtirar) and nature (tab'), so the agent is compelled.
He compares this to losing all causes of an act.
4
Understand the absurd consequence
The Mu'tazilite view forces a situation where one becomes a friend or enemy of Allah under compulsion, which is morally and rationally monstrous.
This shows the theological stakes of the debate.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself how Maturidi uses their own claim about inevitable movement to reinforce his point. This is a common kalam technique: reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما يبين قبح قول المعتزلة عند التحصيل، وإن كان قولهم في الإطلاق قولاً لذيذا في السمع يشبه أن يكون حقا . والله الموفق. وهو أن من قولهم : إن القدرة لا تبقى وقتين وإنها ليست وقت الفعل. فوقع الفعل في الحقيقة ولا قوة له وقت وجوده، وذلك علم الاضطرار ووقوع الفعل بالطبع. ثم الدلالة أن حق مثله الاضطرار [و] أن فقد جميع الأسباب التي بها الفعل لوقته يحيل الفعل ويوصف صاحبه بالاضطرار فوت القدرة التي بها الفعل أحق بذلك ؛ فصيروه مضطرًا إلى ما يصير به وليا الله تعالى، وعدوا له عند الاختيار. ومما يوضح ذلك أيضاً أن من قولهم: إن من أراد التحرك للوقت الثاني منه أنها تقع لا محالة، ولا يقدر صرفها إلا بمنع من قبل غيره. وذلك آية الضرورة. ثم وجبت الولاية والعداوة بمثله، وذلك وحش في العقل.
Then we mention a portion that shows the ugliness of the Mu'tazilites' statement upon verification, although their statement in its absolute sense is a pleasant statement to the ear that seems to be true. Allah is the Granter of success. It is that among their statements: that qudra (power) does not last for two moments and that it is not at the time of the act. So the act occurs in reality while there is no power for it at the time of its existence, and that is the sign of necessity (iḍṭirār) and the occurrence of the act by nature. Then the indication is that the right of its like is necessity, and that the loss of all the causes by which the act exists at its time renders the act impossible, and its possessor is described as compelled, the loss of the power by which the act is more deserving of that; so they made him compelled to that by which he becomes a friend (walī) of Allah, the Exalted, and an enemy to Him at the time of choice. And among that which clarifies this also is that among their statements: that whoever intends movement for the second moment from him, it occurs inevitably, and he cannot turn it away except by prevention from another. That is the sign of necessity. Then loyalty (walāya) and enmity (ʿadāwa) are necessitated by its like, and that is monstrous in the intellect.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that power (qudra) does not persist for two moments and that power is not present at the time of the act. Maturidi begins by noting that the Mu'tazilite statement sounds pleasant but is false upon verification. Their view is that power is not present when the act occurs, so the act happens without power—which is the mark of compulsion (iḍṭirār) and natural necessity. Maturidi argues that losing the causes that enable an act renders the act impossible and the agent compelled. Since the Mu'tazilites say power is lost at the time of the act, they effectively make the agent compelled in becoming a friend or enemy of Allah at the moment of choice. He further clarifies by citing their own claim that if someone intends to move at the next moment, it inevitably occurs unless prevented. This is a sign of necessity, and such a basis for loyalty or enmity is intellectually monstrous (وحش في العقل).

Misconception: That power (qudra) does not need to be present at the time of action for the act to be voluntary and morally responsible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if power is absent, the act occurs by necessity (idtirar) and nature, meaning the agent is compelled and not morally responsible.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite view by showing that denying the persistence of qudra leads logically to compulsion, making moral categories like friendship and enmity to Allah meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi warns that the Mu'tazilite view, though pleasant-sounding, leads to a monstrous conclusion. What pleasant-sounding belief about your own freedom or responsibility have you been comfortably holding, even though deeper reflection might reveal it removes your accountability before Allah? Will you honestly examine it today?
?
If you truly believed that your power to obey or disobey Allah vanished the moment before you acted, would you feel genuinely responsible for your deeds? How would that change your sense of urgency in seeking forgiveness and striving to change? Reflect on a specific sin you have struggled with—does this lesson make you realize you cannot blame a lack of power for your failure?
?
Maturidi shows that denying lasting qudra makes you compelled in the very acts that determine your friendship or enmity with Allah. In what area of your life have you been acting as if you are compelled—by habit, circumstance, or desire—rather than exercising the qudra Allah has given you? What is one concrete step you will take today to reclaim your agency and consciously choose loyalty to Allah?

Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazilite Command and Prohibition
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilite claim that command and prohibition do not apply at the time of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazilite view on command and prohibition at the time of action leads to the invalidation of moral accountability and constitutes an impossible burden.

Core Idea
Command and prohibition must apply at the time of action or moral accountability collapses.

Imam Maturidi engages with a sophisticated Mu'tazilite claim that divine command and prohibition are only metaphorical at the moment of action. He shows that this view logically destroys the very basis of obedience and disobedience, and ultimately amounts to imposing an impossible burden. In this passage, he dissects the Mu'tazilite position and exposes its rational contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the full translation. Note the dense argumentative style.
The passage contains a refutation—track the flow of reasoning.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilites say there is no command or prohibition at the time of the act except metaphorically. Find the sentence that states this.
Look for the phrase 'ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهي إلا على المجاز'.
3
Trace Maturidi's rational principle
Maturidi invokes a rational principle: a command for a future time does not apply now. See how he applies this to the Mu'tazilite infinite regress.
4
Understand the conclusion
The Mu'tazilite view invalidates command and prohibition, and because they deny power at the time of act, it becomes taklif ma la yutaq.
This is the key theological critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن من قولهم : أن ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهي إلا على المجاز كما يقوله المسلمون . وإنما معناه أنه مأمور به بمعنى أن كان من قبل ليفعل فيه . فإذا لم يكن هو مأمورا به ولا منهيا لم يكن بالفعل مؤتمرا ولا مرتكبًا النهي ،وقته وبه تجب العداوة والولاية، فصارا في الحقيقة لا لطاعة ولا لمعصية، أو لا أمر ولا نهى ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى ذلك قولهم: إنه مأمور بالفعل في الوقت الثاني، وفي الثاني مأمور به في الوقت الثالث، كذلك أبدا. فلا يفعل الذي أمر بكل وقت وليس بتارك للأمر، لما ليس بمأمور وقت الترك. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الأصل الذي في العقل دركه أن كل مأمور بالفعل للغَدِ ليس بمأمور هو به للحال، فكذلك [ا] يجب في الوقت القريب؛ فيجب أن الذي أمر بالفعل للوقت الذي يتلوه ليس بمأمور به للحال في العقل . ثم ليس بمأمور به في الوقت الثاني عندهم، ولا منهى عن ضده، فيبطل حقيقة الأمر والنهي بما / في العقل احتماله على قولهم، ويبطل قولهم بما في العقل دفعه . [١٣٩] وهم مع ذلك لا يجعلون له قدرة في ذلك الفعل، فيكون تكليف ما لا يطاق على قولهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also among their statements: that there is no command or prohibition upon him at the time of the act except metaphorically, as the Muslims say. Rather, its meaning is that he is commanded in the sense that he was previously to act in it. So if he is not commanded or prohibited, he is not at the time of the act obedient nor committing the prohibition, at which time loyalty and enmity are necessitated, so they become in reality not for obedience nor for disobedience, or there is no command or prohibition, and there is no power except by Allah. And upon that is their statement: he is commanded for the act in the second moment, and in the second he is commanded for the third moment, and so on forever. So he does not do what he is commanded at each moment, and he is not abandoning the command, because he is not commanded at the time of abandonment. And there is no power except by Allah. Then the principle which the intellect grasps is that everyone commanded for an act tomorrow is not commanded for it now, likewise it is necessary in the near time; so it is necessary that the one who is commanded for an act for the time that follows is not commanded for it at present in the intellect. Then he is not commanded for it in the second moment according to them, nor prohibited from its opposite, so the reality of command and prohibition is invalidated by that which the intellect can conceive according to their statement, and their statement is invalidated by that which the intellect repels. And they also do not ascribe power to him in that act, so it becomes taklīf mā lā yutāq (imposition of what cannot be borne) according to their statement. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that command and prohibition do not apply at the exact time of the act except metaphorically. Maturidi explains that according to the Mu'tazila, the command is understood as having been given earlier, not at the moment of action. Consequently, at the time of the act, the agent is neither obedient nor disobedient, so loyalty or enmity based on the act becomes meaningless. Furthermore, the Mu'tazilites hold that a person is commanded for the next moment, and in that next moment commanded for the following moment, ad infinitum—so they never actually perform the commanded act at the commanded time, nor do they abandon it because they are not commanded at the abandonment time. Maturidi then states the rational principle: a person commanded for tomorrow is not commanded today; similarly, if commanded for the next moment, they are not commanded at the present moment. Since they are not commanded at the second moment according to the Mu'tazilites, nor prohibited from its opposite, the entire reality of command and prohibition collapses. Moreover, since the Mu'tazilites deny power at the time of the act, this amounts to imposing an impossible burden (taklif ma la yutaq).

نهي
مجاز
طاعة
معصية
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies any metaphorical use of command; actually he argues that such a view destroys moral accountability.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny metaphorical command per se, but shows that if command is only metaphorical at the time of action, then obedience and disobedience become meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the Mu'tazilite view on command and prohibition at the time of action lead to?
A) Obedience and disobedience become clearly defined
B) The person is always obedient
C) The reality of command and prohibition is invalidated
D) The person is always disobedient

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazilite view collapses the moral structure of command and prohibition, making it impossible to assign obedience or disobedience and ultimately imposing an impossible burden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay a command of Allah, thinking 'I will do it tomorrow,' are you not effectively treating the command as if it does not apply now? How does Maturidi's argument expose the flaw in such procrastination, and what will you do today to obey a command you have been postponing?
?
Consider a moment when you rationalized a sin by telling yourself 'the prohibition is not active for me right now' or 'I will repent later.' In light of this lesson, how can you ensure that you treat every moment as carrying its own command and prohibition? What specific step will you take this week to break that rationalization?
?
If you truly believed that obedience and disobedience are defined only by an active command at the very moment of action, would you approach your daily choices differently? What is one area of your life where you need to stop living as if the command is only for a future time, and start obeying now?

Maturidi's Principle on Act and Power
Maturidi dismisses a trivial debate and states his principle on act and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that an act performed without power is not truly the agent's act, and how this relates to obligation and incapacity.

Core Idea
An act without power is not truly the agent's act.

Imam Maturidi dismisses a debate between the Mu'tazilites and al-Husayn al-Najjar about whether to call a capacity 'power', calling it meaningless. Instead, he provides a substantive principle: the existence of an act without power for the agent invalidates the act's meaning and transfers it to something else. This lesson unpacks that principle and its implications for obligation and incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argument flow come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's concise style.
2
Identify the dispute being dismissed
The passage starts by saying the dispute with al-Husayn has no meaning. Understand what that dispute is about: whether a disbeliever's capacity for obedience is called 'power' or not.
Maturidi thinks this is just a naming issue.
3
Understand the core principle
Maturidi states: 'the existence of the act while there is no power for the one who has the act upon him invalidates the meaning of the act and transfers it to something else.' This is the key point. Also, an act from an ignorant person is impermissible.
This principle links act, power, and agency.
4
Examine the parallel with obligation and incapacity
Maturidi draws a parallel: just as obligation (khitab) is based on knowledge, power is required for responsibility. The incapable (ajiz) is not obligated because he lacks the means of capacity, just as the insane are not obligated due to lacking knowledge.
This reinforces that moral responsibility requires both knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المسألة بينهم وبين الحسين لا معنی لها، لأن الحسين يقول : كل شيء يكون به فعل الطاعة كان مع الكافر سوى العصمة والتوفيق. وهم وافقوه في أنه لا يوصف بعصمة ولا توفيق، فحصل اختلافهم على تسميته قوة أو لا . ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الأصل عندنا في المسألة أن وجود الفعل ولا قوة لمن له الفعل عليه يُبطل معنى الفعل ويصرفه إلى غيره، وكذلك وجود الفعل ممن هو جاهل به، وهو غير جائز . ثم كان الخطاب لازما بسبب العلم ، وإن لم يكن حقيقته مما لو طلب يُظفر به فكذلك القدرة والعاجز الذي لا يلزمه الكلفة لفوت ما به يطاق، كما لا يلزم المجنون لفوت ما به يعلم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the question between them and al-Husayn has no meaning, because al-Husayn says: everything by which the act of obedience occurs is present with the disbeliever except al-ʿiṣma and al-tawfīq. And they agreed with him that he is not described with ʿiṣma nor tawfīq, so their disagreement amounts to naming it power or not. And there is no power except by Allah. Then the principle with us in the matter is that the existence of the act while there is no power for the one who has the act upon him invalidates the meaning of the act and transfers it to something else, and likewise the existence of the act from one who is ignorant of it is not permissible. Then discourse (khitāb) is necessary because of knowledge, even if its reality is not such that if sought it could be attained; so too is power and the incapable (ʿājiz) who is not obliged with burden (kulfa) due to the loss of what enables power, just as the insane person is not obliged due to the loss of what enables knowledge. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a dismissal of the dispute between the Mu'tazilites and al-Husayn (likely al-Najjar) regarding power. Al-Husayn claims that a disbeliever has everything needed for obedience except 'isma and tawfiq, and the Mu'tazilites agree that the disbeliever is not described with these. The only disagreement is whether to call that remaining capacity 'power' or not. Maturidi says this is trivial. He then states his own principle: if an act exists without power for the agent, the meaning of the act is invalidated and attributed to something else. Similarly, an act from an ignorant person is impermissible. He then parallels obligation (khitab) with power: just as obligation is based on knowledge (even if that knowledge is not directly attainable), power is required for obligation. The incapable person (ajiz) is not obligated because he lacks the means of capacity, just as the insane person is not obligated because he lacks the means of knowledge. This reinforces the link between capacity, knowledge, and moral responsibility.

Key Terms
حسين
عصمة
توفيق
قوة
عاجز
مجنون
خطاب
كلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi rejects all value in the debate over naming, implying that the entire discussion is worthless.
Correction: Maturidi dismisses only the trivial naming dispute, but he then provides a substantive principle that grounds moral responsibility in the presence of power and knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's principle in this passage, what invalidates the meaning of an act?
A) The act being from a disbeliever
B) The act occurring without power for the agent
C) The act being an act of obedience
D) The act being commanded

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle: an act performed without power is not truly the agent's act; similarly, incapability (like ignorance) lifts moral obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever claim credit for an act that you know you did not truly have the power to perform? How does Maturidi's principle challenge your sense of ownership over your deeds?
?
When you fail to obey, do you honestly examine whether you lacked the power or knowledge needed, or do you simply blame yourself? What would it mean to accept that some failures are not truly yours?
?
If the insane are not held accountable because they lack knowledge, how does that affect your view of those with mental illness? Are you quick to judge others without considering their capacity? What would it take for you to extend that same grace to yourself?
